This report contains individual evaluations of each intermediary.
An identical sequence of presentation has been followed for the eight
financieras in Appendices A-H. An appendix letter identifies each page of
text and annex.
For ease of reference, the basic data sheet from the Appraisal
Report has been reproduced on the following page. It provides some basic
information on each of the financieras for purposes of conparison and
contains preliminary 1972 financial data. While major developments in
1972-73 are reflected in the text, actual financial statements for 1972
have not been included in the annexes, as audited figures are not yet
available.

APPENDIX A
Page 1
CORPORACION FINANCIERA COLOMBIANA
INTRODUCTION
1.        Colombiana, the oldest and still leading financiera, was established
in 1959 and began operations in the same year. Although it concentrates its
activities in the Bogota area, which is the country's most industrialized, it
is also active in most of the other Departments of Colombia. IFC became a
shareholder as early as 1961.
ORGANIZATION
2.        Share Capital and Ownership.  Colombiana's share capital increased
only 1.5% to Col$139.8 million during 1971, and to Col$144.0 million in 1972.
New subscriptions were made entirely by Colombiana private shareholders. Own-
ership (Annex 1) changed little.  At the end of 1971, priPrate Colombian share-
holders owned 53.7% of the total, foreign and foreign-controlled shareholders,
32.0%, IFC, 14.0%, and two governmental shareholders 0.3%. Colombiana con-
tinued to be widely owned by 203 shareholders, of which Banco de Bogota and
Colseguros, jointly, held about one third of the total. Banco de Bogota with
18.75% had remained the largest shareholder, followed by IFC.
3.        Board of Directors and Management.  Colombiana's Board of Directors,
(Annex 2) which comprises leading Colombian and foreign bankers and business-
men, has continued to meet weekly and to follow closely Colomoiana's operations.
In April 1971, Dr. Luis Machado, IFC's Consultant, was appoirnted Advisor to
Colombiana's Board.
4.        No changes have occurred in Colombiana's management.  Mr. Ignacio
Copete has been President since 1967. He has an impressive professional
record and manages the financiera efficiently.  He is assisted effectively
by three Vice-Presidents.
5.        Staff and Internal Organization.  The financiera's organization
(Annex 3) is adequate, given its present level of operations. Colombiana's
professional staff, which continues to be large compared with other financie-
ras, seems to be the most competent of all financieras, on balance. Internal
procedures and management reporting are satisfactory.
6.        Appraisal and Follow-up.  Colombiana's appraisal work continues to
be of generally good quality. Coverage of engineering asnects by a capable
team of engineers is adequate. Colombiana has become most familiar with the
technique and interpretation of the effective protection calculation which
all financieras introduced in connection with the last Bank loan. Colombiana's
supervision system is well designed.  Powever, actual supervision work in
1971 was relatively poor as staff efforts concentrated on preparing sub-projects
for submission under the last Bank loan, at the expense of follow-up which has
tended to be limited to Colombiana's largest exposures, problem cases and firms



APPENDIX A
Page 2
in the construction stage. Management, being aware of the need for improved
supervision employed five new staff members in 1972 who, once they have been
trained, should permit Colombiana to do a satisfactory follow-up job.
7.        Procurement and Disbursement Procedures.  Colombiana, like the other
financieras, largely relies on its clients for proper equipment selection.
Only in the case of larger projects does it require its clients to present
quotations from different suppliers.  Disbursement for imported goods follow
adequate standardized procedures and internal control of disbursements is
satisfactory. However, disbursement control could be improved through more
frequent site visits.
OPERATI ONS
8.        Lending Operations.  A break-down of peso lending operations, in-
cluding loans for import financing with Bank funds, is given in Annex 4. As
Colombiana has approved the largest portion of all financieras under the latest
Bank loan, the total loan approvals almost doubled in 1971 to Col$674.0 million.
Most of the approvals (95.2%) have been for manufacturing, with textiles/
apparel, non-metallic minerals and metals/engineering receiving the largest
share; approvals are well diversified among industrial sectors. Reflecting
the increasing portion of Bank-financed projects in 1971, loans approved were
larger than those of 1970--80% against 60% exceeded Col$5 million--loans for
fixed capital financing went up from 51% to 68% and loan maturities of more
than five years increased from 50% to 68%.  The geographical distribution of
Colombiana's lending has remained the most widespread among the financieras;
46% of its 1971 loan approvals involved clients outside its home Department
of Cundinamarca. Reflecting partly the increasing volume of Bank-financed
projects, Colombiana's foreign exchange financing, in particular short-term
export and import financing, grew substantially in 1971, from US$4.9 million
to US$7.9 million.
9.        Equity Investments and Promotions.  Colombiana's equity portfolio
(Annex 5) increased by 7.0% to Col$176.1 million in 1971. As of the end of
1971, equity investments equalled 69.7% of Colombiana's own equity, compared
with 68.3% a year earlier. The main new investments involved Col$30.9 million
in Celanese Colombiana, and Col$5.7 million in Acabados y Estampados Tintorex
(both operating profitably). Investments of Col$9.0 million in Forjas de
Colombia (in liquidation) and a Col$12.0 million portion of an investment in
El Labrador (operating at a loss) were sold at fractions of cost.  The remainder
of the El Labrador investment was exchanged for shares of Col$6.5 million in a
new company continuing one line of El Labrador's operations. The financiera's
Policy Statement limits its holdings normally to 25% of any one company's
equity and its total exposure in any one company to 25% of its own equity.
Equity investments in excess of the first limit involve ten companies. Al-
though Colombiana intends to decrease its exposure in most of these companies,
sales of shares are difficult for lack of maturity of the investments and the
thin equity market. Only Colombiana's total exposure in Cales y Cementos de
Toluvitjo (Tolcemento), amounting to 71.7% of the financiera's equity, exceeded



APPENDIX A
Page 3
the second limit as of end 1971.  However, although Colombiana's total exposure
has remained unchanged, the financiera has been able to sell since then most of
its equity investment in the company thereby decreasing loss prospects (see
para. 14). In its earlier years of operations, Colombiana was active in
promoting new enterprises. Since 1967 priority was rightly given to the con-
solidation of portfolio. In 1971, Colombiana played a substantial role in
the promotion and consolidation of Acabados y Estampados Tintorex. Colombiana
is reviewing several investment possibilities, particularly in connection with
markets operated through the Andean Pact, which may lead to equity investments.
10.       Other Pperations.  Together with Valle and IFC, Colombiana partici-
pated in the underwqriting of a Col$8.6 million share issue of Pro-hoteles,.of
which more than 80% has been placed. Colombiana has somewhat decreased its
guarantee operations and commitments. Guarantees outstanding, at the end of
1971 were Col$84.3 million, compared to Col$97.7 million a year earlier, the
drop being largely due to converting guarantees for Tolcemento into loans.
FINANCIAL POSITION AND RESULTS
11.       Borrowings and Resource Position.  In 1971, Colombiana's growth of
total assets was only a modest 7.6%, compared to 13.5% in 1972. The 1971
increase was financed as shown below (in Col$ million):
Dec6mber 31, 1970   December 31, 1971  Increase/
% of                  % of   (decrease)
Amount      Total    Amount       Total Amount   %
IBRD                             188.1        17.1      213.1      18.0    25.0  29.9
Own equity                       234.0        21.2      241.6      20.4      7.6   9.1
Br, lines                        338.5        30.7      355.6     3.0.0    17.1  20.5
Foreign commercial banks          73.6         6.6      119.7      10.1    46.1  55.1
Bonds                            141.9        12.9      161.8      13.6    19.9  23.8

BR lines continued to be Colombiana's single most important source of finance
and accounted for 30.0% of total resources at the end of 1971. Own equity
although dropping from 21.2% in 1970 to 20.4% of the total, remained second in
importance.  Use of Bank funds increased by a relatively low 13.3%, accounting
for 18.0% of total resources at the end of 1971. Mainly by selling bonds to
institutional investors, who are shareholders of Colombiana, the company has
been the most successful financiera in raising local resources in the bond
market. Although, as of 1971, 13.6% of total resources came from bond place-
ments, Colombiana, like the other financieras, continues to be faced with a
tight local currency position. Ilowever, the five financieras have been making
great effort to raise additional local resources by selling bonds jointly.



APPENDIX A
Page 4
Finally, the increasing utilization of short-term credit lines from foreign
commercial banks reflects wide fluctuations, which have also occurred in the
past, rather than a trend.
12.       Financial Structure.  Historic financial statements, covering fiscal
years 1967-71 (Annex 6), show that Colombiana has maintained a sound overall
financial structure. The financiera's total debt/equity ratio increased from
4.1:1 in 1970, to 4.3:1 in 1971, making allowance for estimated prudent
provisions (see para. 16) in both years, and to 4.6:1 in 1972, thus approaching
the maximum of 5:1, stipulated in the Loan Agreements with the Bank and in
Colombiana's own Policy Statements.  Colombiana's short-term liquidity sit-
uation is satisfactory; short-term assets and the normal monthly cash flow
cover short-term liabilities comfortably.
13.       Income, Expenses and Profitability.  Colombiana's operating results
have been satisfactory. Its net profit increased by 13.8% from Col$31.3 nil-
lion in 1971 to Col$35.6 million in 1972 and return, both on average paid-in
capital (25.6%) and on average equity (13.7%) improved accordingly. The over-
whelming part of Colombiana's income is derived from lending operations. Ad-
ministrative costs continue to be high compared with Nacional, a development
finance company comparable in size and age. Colombiana has, however, built
up a large and competent staff which should be able to handle the expected
substantially larger volume of business in the future.
14.       Quality of Loan and Investment Portfolios.  Colombiana's loan and
guarantee portfolio is intrinsically sound.  Of the year-end 1971 total, more
than two thirds were outstanding to companies operating profitably, over one
quarter to companies operating at a loss (of which Tolcemento accounted for
almost one half), and Col$5.1 million to three companies in liquidation. Total
principal and interest of clients in arrears for more than three months amounted
to Col$43.2 million, whereas the total amount outstanding with these clients
was Col$57.8 million, or 5.3% of the total portfolio of loans and guarantees
extended. Although this is not excessive, Colombiana should continue giving
attention to improving its collection record. Colombiana is well secured
in most cases and apparently has more than ample guarantees for the loans
to companies in liquidation. The financiera's management considers that
Col$8.5 million is adequate to provide for possible losses on doubtful loans.
15.       As of end 1971, the quality of Colombiana's equity portfolio (Annex
5) contained a considerable element of risk, mainly because of the financiera's
substantial and increasing exposure (equal to about three quarters of its own
equity) in Tolcemento, a cement company well known to the Bank Group, which
has incurred increasingly high losses and whose prospects of turning around
do not seem to be clear. Colombiana's auditors qualified their opinion
regarding equity investments of Col$68.7 million, or 39% of the total equity
investments, in six companies in pre-operation or operating at a loss, of
which Tolcemento accounted for almost three quarters. Colombiana's total
year-pnd 1971 exposure (investments plus loans and guarantees) in these



APPENDIX A
Page 5
companies amounted to Col$210.9 million, of which Col$173.2 million referred
to the exposure of Tolcemento. In 1972, however, except for Col$8.7 million,
Colombiana was able to sell its equity investment in Tolcemento at par to a
competing cement producer. As conditions of the sale the financiera had to
convert an additional Col$34.7 million of loans into equity, and to finance
the entire purchasing price of Col$75.2 million at concessionary terms.  As
a result of this transaction 1/ Colombiana's total exposure in Tolcemento
has remained unchanged, but the inherent risk of losses in Colombiana's portfolio
has been substantially reduced, at the fair cost of an interest subsidy which
will affect the financiera's income over the 14-year term of the loan to the
purchaser. Of Colombiana's other large exposures in companies in pre-operation
or operating at a loss, prospects of Grasas del Litoral and Palmeras de la
Costa, both agricultural enterprises, continuing mainly El Labrador's pro-
duction lines, remain unclear. So are the prospects of Tennessee Colombia/
Petroleos Nacionales, operating in the risky field of oil exploration. Pro-
spects of Pro-hoteles and V. Burrowes Industrias are discussed in para. 15
of Appendix C. Overall, with the sale of shares in Tolcemento, Forjas de
Colombia and El Labrador (para. 9) the quality of Colombiana's equity port-
folio has improved. Equity write-offs in 1971-72 were insignificant.
16.       Reserves and Dividend Policy.  Like the other financieras, Colombiana
does not have provisions for portfolio losses. However, the 1971 audit report
separated for the first time from Colombiana's equity reserves for possible
losses of Col$16.3 million 2/, indicating thereby the level of provisions
which, in the opinion of Colombiana's Board, would be sufficient to cover
possible losses on the realization of loans and investments. Excluding this
amount and dividends declared out of 1971 earnings, Colombiana had total
reserves and retained earnings of Col$85.0 million, equivalent to 7.6% of
total loan and equity portfolio; Colombiana allocated 45.4% of 1972 earnings
to reserves. Overall, reserves appear adequate given the present quality of
Colombiana's loan and equity portfolio, but as the financiera grows it should
continue to strengthen its reserves position.
17.       Colombiana's shares are not listed at either of the Colombian stock
exchanges. During 1971, Colombiana placed some 200,000 shares with investors
under the economic development reserve at 125% of par; reported private trans-
actions, however, did not exceed par. This compares with a book value of
160.8% of par at the end of 1971. The low price is a reflection of the
financiera's poor dividend record which, in turn, is a result of the need to
build up substantive portfolio reserves. Since profitability is expected to
1/   Not reflected in the projections in Annex 7.
2/   Col$8.5 million for doubtful loans and Col$7.8 million for equity
investments.



APPENDIX A
Page 6
continue to improve, Colombiana should be able to gradually increase its
dividends. However, return to investors will probably not become attractive
enough for some time to create a significant market for Colombiana shares.
Out of 1972 earnings, Colombiana declared a 13.2% dividend, representing a
53.4% pay-out, compared to 12% and 53.6%, and 12% and 59.4%, respectively,
in 1971 and 1970. Reflecting a higher level of necessary provisions in 1971,
as indicated by the auditors' report, book value of shares increased only
marginally from 157.8% to 160.7% of par. Earnings per share rose faster,
from Col$2.01 to Col$2.24 and to Col$2.47 in 1972, which, however, is still
low compared to Nacional and Valle.
18.       Foreign Exchange Risk.  In all cases examined by the mission, the
financiera had followed its policy of not incurring the foreign exchange risks.
19.       Audit of Accounts.  Colombiana's accounts are audited annually by
Price Waterhouse & Co. whose 1971 report contains a qualification with respect
to the realization value of approximately Col$68 million in equity investments
(see para. 15).
PROSPECTS
20.       Business Forecasts.  Colombiana's forecasts (Annex 7) of operations
for 1972-76 assume rather modest rates of growth. Local currency loan appro-
vals, after a drop of 431% in 1972 over the unusually high level of approvals
in 1971 (due to Colombiana's very large participation under the last Bank
Loan) are projected to grow at an annual rate of 11% from 1973 to 1976.
Foreign currency loan approvals, mainly import financing in connection with
the last Bank Loan, would increase by 23% in 1972, then level off at an
average annual growth rate of 8%. New equity investments would drop from
Col$51.7 million in 1971 to Col$34.5 million in 1972, mainly in line with
previously made commitments. As no such commitments exist beyond 1972, new
equity investments from 1973 through end 1976 are projected very conservatively
between Col$4.5-5.0 million annually. Considering the expected continuing
strong demand for industrial finance and Colombiana's ample project evaluation
capacity, these projections should be easily attainable, if Colombiana can
obtain the expected resources for financing of equipment imports. Colombiana's
projections also indicate that the financiera intends to further consolidate
its portfolio by taking a conservative approach to equity investments.
21.       Sources of Funds.  The projected increases in resources assume sub-
stantial Bank support as well as fresh funds from PIF, IFF and BR. Colombiana
also expects to use extensivelv foreign commercial bank lines for export-import
financing. Projected commitments of Bank funds are partly based on a pipeline
of projects which are either under study by Colombiana or for which definite
investment proposals are expected. Projected bond placements seem somewhat
conservative. Considering the success of joint bond issues in 1972, Colombiana
should well exceed the projected targets throughout 1976. By attracting sub-
scriptions mainly from Colombian enterprises, Colombiana expects to increase



APPENDIX A
Page 7
its shlare capital by Col$60 million to reach Col$200 million by 1976.  This
may be difficult to achieve. Overall, however, Colombiana's projections
may result to be conservative. Developments in 1972 support this assumption.
22.       Financial Prolections.  In line with its expected business, Colom-
biana's total assets would grow at an annual average rate of about 10%, from
Col$1,186.4 million in 1971 to Col$1,954.1 million in 1976, which compares
wiith annual growth rates of total assets of 7.6%"' in 1971 and 24.2% in 1970.
Since equity investments would expand more slowly than loans--and expected
increases in owm equity--from Col$168.3 million in 1971 to Col$222.3 million
in 1976, Colombiana's equity portfolio as a proportion of its net worth would
decrease from 69.7% in 1971 to 54.6% in 1976. Due to rather substantial capital
increases expected in the period 1972-74, Colombiana's indebtedness would
remain within its present debt/equity limitation of 5:1, even dropping from
4.3:1 in 1971 to 4.1:1 in 1976. As Colonbiana may not be able to raise the
projected additional capital and to provide an incentive for higlher indebted-
ness through local resource mobilization beyond its present limitation on
leverage, Colorbiana's contractual debt/equity ratio would be relaxed to 6:1.
Colombiana's operating experience, the level of its provisions and improvement
of the quiality of its portfolio provide an adequate basis for this increase.
23.       Reserves and retained earnings (including total annual net income)
are expected to increase slightly from 8.6% of total portfolio in 1971 to
10.5% in 197T, which should allow adequate portfolio coverage. Projected
liquidity, expected to improve somewhat, would remain sufficient. Throughout
the forecast period debt-service coverage is projected to remain adequate.
24.       If Colombiana can achieve the expected volume of business and
financial margins, its net profit xwould almost double, from Col$31.3 million
in 1971 to Col$56.5 million in 1976. Rates of returns on average equity would
increase from 13.2% in 1971 to 14.3% ir 1976. Increasing profitability is also
reflected in dividends, expected to rise slowly from 12.0% of par in 1°71 to
15.6% of par in 1976, but within the limits of prudent pay-out ratios not
exceeding 55, of net profits.  Ratios of return on eauity and dividends may
be higher than projected, depending on the actual capital additions. Although
Colombiana expects its administrative costs to increase from 1.5% of average
total assets in 1971 to 1.6% in 1976, the financiera should be able to lower
these costs to a level comparable to the one projected for 1976 by Nacional
and Valle, of 0.9% and 1.3%, respectively.
CONCLIJSIONS AND RECOMfENDATIONS
25.       Colonbiana is the larrest and oldest of the financieras.  Overall,
it has performed creditablv. By providing industrial financing on a national
scale it has made a substantial contribution to Colombia's econoric develop-
ment and has played an important role within Colombia's economy. The leading role
it has taken in promoting enterprises had led to large exposures of consider-
able risk. However, Colombiana has successfully continued its prudent policy



APPENDIX A
Page 8
of consolidation, initiated in 1967. As a result, the financiera has gradually
improved the overall quality of its equity portfolio and its profitability.
26.       Colombiana's competent management has continued to strengthen the
financiera's organization to a rather efficient level. Its ample staff has,
perhaps, the most diversified skills among all financieras. Colombiana's
appraisal work is of good quality, whereas its supervision leaves roonm for
improvement.
27.       Colombiana has made effective use of Bank funds.  Its debt-service
capacity is expected to remain adequate. On the basis of its performance,
the financiera continues to be creditworthy and is a suitable recipient for
the proposed loan. In consideration of Colombiana's operating experience,
the improvement in its performance and the strength of its portfolio and
reserves, it is recommended that the 5:1 debt/equity limitation provided for
in the Subsidiary Loan Agreements under the Bank Loans be relaxed to 6:1,
and that Colombianals Statement of Policies and Procedures be modified ac-
cordingly. Colombiana agreed to include necessary provisions in the debt/equity
ratio computation.

APPENDIX B
Page 1
CORPORACION FINANCIERA NACIONAL
INTRODUCTION
1.        Nacional was founded in 1959 and started operating in 1960 in
Medellin, the capital of the Department of Antioquia, which has the second
largest concentration of industry in Colombia. IFC made an investment in
Nacional as early as 1961.
ORGANIZATION
2.        Share Capital and Ownership.  At December 31, 1972, Nacional had
a paid-in share capital of Col$147.0 million, up a substantial Col$17.3
million or 13.3%, from the end of 1971.  Additional subscriptions were mainly
made by existing and new Colombian private shareholders. At year-end 1971
Nacional was owned by 183 shareholders (Annex 1), compared to 146 a year
earlier; with only one stockholder, Banco Comercial Antioqueno, holding more
than 10%. Private Colombian shareholders accounted for 62.8% of this total,
foreign or foreign controlled firms 25.3%, Government bodies 3.7% and IFC
8.2%.
3.        Board of Directors and Management.  Nacional's 10-member Board of
of Directors (Annex 2) seven directors represent Colombian shareholders,
three foreign interests. IFC is not represented. However, Dr. Luis Machado,
IFC's Consultant, has been advisor to Nacional's Board since mid-1971.
Nacional continues to take the most conservative approach of the five fi-
nancieras to development banking, often relying more heavily on security
presented for loans than the merits of project analysis.
4.        No changes have taken place in Nacional's internal management.
Mr. Jose Gutierrez has been the financieras' President since the beginning
of its operations.  He provides the financiera with forceful leadership.  Its
Vice-President is competent and a valuable complement to the President. How-
ever, for a company the size of Nacional, it lacks depth of management. In
particular, employment of a Technical Vice-President is becoming a matter of
priority. Nacional agreed to make beat efforts to fill the vacancy as soon
as possible.
5.        Staff and Internal Organization.  Nacional's organization (Annex
3), is adequate. The financiera's internal system of reporting and controls
seems to be adequate and to work quite well. Nacional's limited staff has
been hard pressed to handle the increasing volume of business. To a large
part Nacional has been able to operate with such a small staff because most
of its clients have been the larger and well-established firms, requiring
little assistance from the financiera to prepare investment proposals. If
Nacional is to reach, to a greater extent than it has in the past, medium-sized
and less well-established firms that may not have the expertise of its present



APPENDIX B
Page 2
clients, the financiera's professional staff will have to be expanded. Nacional
agreed to hire one more financial analyst and one more engineer to strengthen
its appraisal and supervision capacity for the proposed loan.
6.        Appraisal and Follow-up.  The quality of Nacional's appraisal work
has shown some improvement, mainly in the financial and market analysis areas.
Recent project appraisals indicate that Nacional's evaluations have become
more thorough, although it still tends to rely substantially on proposals
received from large and well-established firms with considerable experience.
Nacional has become sufficiently familiar with the effective protection
calculation, a procedure for economic project evaluation which was agreed
upon with the Bank in connection with the last loan. Engineering analysis
remains a deficiency in its appraisal work. Presently, the financiera has
only one engineer with limited experience and insufficient time for thorough
evaluations. In 1971, Nacional as the first and still only financiera em-
ployed an economist to investigate export possibilities for Colombian manu-
factured products as a service for its clients and to evaluate export poten-
tials of investment proposals. Results of this experiment have been encouraging.
7.        Nacional's follow-up work still leaves much to be desired, mmainly
due to professional staff shortages. Supervision is inadequate from a
technical aspect, as emphasis is placed on supervising the credit rather
than the project. In maniy cases there is no formal supervision, simply
reliance on an informal relationship with clients or on the prestige of the
borrower. However, through the presence of major regional banks on its
Board, the proximity of many clients to its headquarters and close informal
contacts between them and its staff, Nacional has been able to close much of
the information gap regarding current financial position and prospects of
clients. The additional staff Nacional intends to employ should bring im-
provement in its supervision.
8.        Procurement and Disbursement Procedures.  Nacional leaves procure-
ment largely to its clients; little checking is done with regard to the
competitiveness of prices or the appropriateness of the equipment. With its
present clientele of largely experienced firms this is not an unreasonable
procedure. However, if Nacional is to expand its financial services more to
medium-sized firms, procurement procedures will have to be markedly strength-
ened. Disbursement procedures are well conceived and provide for adequate
checks, although disbursement control for construction work could be improved
through more frequent site visits.
OPERATIONS
9.        Lending Operations.  At the end of 1971, Nacional's total outstand-
ing loan portfolio was Col$679.8 million, 1/ an advance of 24.7% from a year
earlier. In 1971, it disbursed 300 peso loans, totalling Col$288.9 mmillion,
1/   Total loan and export-import financing portfolio rose to Col$869.8 mil-
lion at the end of 1972.



APPENDIX B
Page 3
a 19.2% increase over 1970 disbursements (Annex 4). Nacional had continued
to concentrate its lending in the Department of Antioquia, where it is
located; the drop from 92.2% to 74.7% of the total lending to this region in
1971 was due to Nacional's participation in several large joint projects with
other financieras. The share of the sub-sectors food, beverages and tobacco,
textiles and apparel and paper and printing further increased to over 60%,
reflecting, in part, the distribution of local manufacturing. In 1971, work-
ing capital financing accounted for 53.3% of the total disbursements and loans
of up to five years for 56.7% of the total, as against 48.8% and 52.9% re-
spectively. This can be explained by Nacional's relatively small participa-
tion under the last Bank loan. As might be expected, given Nacional's con-
servative business approach, as in the past almost all loans disbursed in 1971
were for existing enterprises. Nacional has not felt the need to engage sub-
stantially in short-term export-import financing since management feels that
the financiera should not compete with shareholder banks which adequately meet
existing needs. Outstanding foreign exchange loans increased from US$1.0 mil-
lion to US$1.5 million equivalent in 1971.
10.       Equity Investments and Promotions.  In 1971, Nacional's gross equity
investments (Annex 5) increased somewhat slower than its own equity, by 13.1%
from Col$96.8 million to Col$109.5 million (and to Col$120.0 million at the
end of 1972). Thus, at the end of 1971, equity portfolio came to 53.1% of
Nacional's own equity, down slightly from 54.5% for 1970.  An initial investment
in one company, Comestibles La Rosa, accounted for more than 70% of the 1971
increase. Small initial investments were made in three other companies, and
investments were increased in six firms already in Nacional's equity portfolio.
Nacional sold part of its shares in two firms, Industrias Metalicas de Palmira
at a capital gain of Col$730,000, and Productos Para El Hogar at cost. Ac-
cording to Nacional's Policy Statement, the financiera's equity holding of
an individual firm would normally not exceed 25% of the subscribed capital
of that firm. Nacional has continued to exceed this limit in Electroquimica
Colombiana (45.3%), Industrias Metalicas de Palmira (33.5%), and Ganadera
Nacional (28.6%). All three companies are operating profitably and are paying
dividends; the risk of losses or excessive management involvement seems,
therefore, small.
11.       Nacional's promotional activities since its inception have been
rather limited. None of its investments made in 1971 was in companies pro-
moted predominantly by Nacional. However, indications are that the financiera
may intend to become more promotion-minded, as evidenced by its creation of
in-house capacity to investigate export opportunities for Colombian producers.
In the context of opportunities arising from the Andean Common Market, Nacional
is considering to take the lead in reorganizing and expanding several enter-
prises. The projections foresee a substantial increase of equity investments.
12.       Other Operations.  Nacional has not acted as an underwriter for
securities, which reflects both its conservative business attitude and the
depressed state of the Colombian equity market in recent years. The



APPENDIX B
Page 4
financiera extended no new guarantees in 1971; guarantees outstanding re-
mained at an insignificant Col$8 million.
FINANCIAL POSITION AND RESULTS
13.       Borrowings and Resource Position.  During 1971 Nacional's total
assets increased by 22.0% from Col$666.3 million to Col$812.8 million, which
compares to a 27.4% increase, to Col$1,036.3 million in 1972. The 1971 in-
crease was financed as shown below (in Col$ million):

The composition of resources utilized by Nacional has changed relatively
little in 1971. IBRD credit lines have somewhat increased their importance
as Nacional's single most important source of finance, followed closely by
funds provided from BR lines and Nacional's own equity. These three sources
provided almost nine tenths of the total resources. Resources raised through
Nacional's own bonds from institutional investors increased faster than total
resources, but still only provided 2.7% of total resources at the end of 1971.
Nacional's local and foreign currency resource situation, as those of other
financieras, was tight at the end of 1972. Assured resources for term lend-
ing to finance fixed assets lending were practically fully committed, and
local resource availability was essentially limited to funds from BR and re-
payments and cash generation from the financiera's operations. However, the
five financieras have been successful in raising additional local resources
through joint bond issues in 1972.
14.       Financial Structure.  Annex 6 gives summary financial statements
for 1967-71. The financiera's financial structure reflects the conservative
policy of management, as Nacional has been operating well within its 5:1 debt/
equity limitation. Leverage on equity has increased only slightly from 2.6:1
in 1970 to 2.9:1 in 1971 and 3.1:1 in 1972. The 1971 ratio remains unchanged
even if allowance is made for prudent provisions (see para. 18). The propor-
tion of equity portfolio to total portfolio has declined slightly, reflecting
management interest in lending operations, and was equal to 13.9% of total
portfolio ccompared to 15.1% a year earlier. Although its current ratio
decreased somewhat during 1971, from 1.6:1 to 1.4:1, Nacional has maintained
good iiquidity.



Ai'PENDIX B
Page 5
15.       Income, Expenses and Profitability.  Nacional receives a larger
return on its equity than any of the other financieras. Return on equity
investments increased from 11.6% to 12.6% of average equity portfolio con-
tributing a satisfactory 10.2% and 10.3%, respectively, to total income.
Nacional continues to be the most profitable financiera in absolute and in
relative terms. Net profit increased from Col$30.4 million in 1970 to
Col$36.9 million in 1971 and Col$43.5 million in 1972. Profit before tax
on average total assets rose from 6.9% to 7.0%, and net profit on average
share capital increased from 28.3% to 30.0% and 31.4% in 1972. Increased
profitability is mainly due to higher leverage and a further cut-down of
administrative cost to 1.0% of average total assets, by far the lowest
among the financieras.
16.       Quality of Loan and Investment Portfolio.  Nacional's loan portfolio
is sound with arrearage continuing to be minimal. As of end 1971, principal
and interest in arrears for more than three months involving three clients
amounted to Col$1.5 million; the total outstanding with these clients in ar-
rears was Col$20.2 million, equivalent to 3.0% of the financiera's total loan
portfolio. By mid-1972, two of these clients had completely repaid their
loans and the third one was paying regularly. In no case does Nacional's
total exposure to any firm exceed 18% of its own equity. Loans are heavily
concentrated in two industrial sub-sectors, food, beverages and tobacco, and
textiles and apparels; but given their past performance and future prospects,
Nacional's concentration in these sub-sectors does not diminish the high
quality of its loan portfolio. Nacional refinanced no loans during 1971.
Management expects no significant losses from the financiera's loan portfolio.
17.       Nacional's equity portfolio (Annex 5) totalling Col$109.5 million
is of high quality, with about nine-tenths of the total in companies operating
at a profit. Of the 29 companies in which Nacional has holdings, 22 are operat-
ing at a profit, three in development or pre-operating stages, and four are
operating at a loss. Except for Forjas, which is in the process of liquida-
tion, the financiera expects no losses. Of the companies in development or
operating at a loss, prospects of the oil exploration company Petroleos
Nacionales remain unclear; Promotora de Hoteles de Turismo is operating an
international hotel in Medellin. At present it suffers fron insufficient cash
flow, due to low rates of occupancy, but the hotel is expected to be viable
in the long run. Nacional's investments are concentrated in seven companies
which account for 70.4% of its equity portfolio. All these companies, except
for Forjas, are operating profitably.
18.       Reserves and Dividend Policy.  Like the other financieras, Nacional
has no specific provisions for portfolio losses. However, in the 1971 audit,
the auditors did not include in the financiera's equity the Col$7.4 million
reserve for losses on investments, practically equal to its total investment
in Forjas, thus indicating the level of provisions which in the opinion of
Nacional's management would be sufficient to cover possible losses from equity
portfolio. Given the outstanding quality of Nacional's loan portfolio, no



APPENDIX B
Page 6
provisions for losses from it seem necessary at this time. At the end of
1971, excluding the above estimated provisions and dividends declared out of
1971 earnings, Nacional had total reserves of Col$63.5 million, equivalent
to 8.3% of total loan and equity portfolio. This is adequate given the good
quality of the portfolio.
19.       Following a dividend policy which gives high priority to making the
financiera's shares marketable above par and raising investors' interest in
them, Nacional has achieved the best dividend record of all financieras. Its
shares are the only financiera stock traded on the Colombian stock exchanges.
Due to the recently depressed state of the stock market, however, trading in
Nacional's shares was very thin and prices (120% of par for latest known trans-
action in August 1972), were substantially below book value, in spite of com-
petitive dividends. Out of 1972 earnings a 22.8% dividend was declared,
equivalent to a 77%, profit pay-out, following a 21.6% dividend and a 76% pay-
out ratio for 1971. These high pay-outs have been prudent in the light of
the high quality of the financiera's portfolio and its adequate reserves.
Mainly as a result of the substantial capital increase in 1921, book value
of shares increased only marginally from 148.77 to 150.8%, and earnings per
share, from Col$2.91 to Col$3.09.
20.       Foreign Exchange Risk.  All operations reviewed by the mission in-
dicate that the financiera is not exposed to the risk of incurring foreign
exchange losses.
21.       Audit of Accounts.  The financiera's accounts are audited annually
by Price W4aterhouse and Co., whose opinion for 1971 was given without quali-
fication.
PROSPECTS
22.       Business Forecasts.  In its 5-year forecasts (Annex 7), Nacional
has used a realistic approach, basing its assumptions mainly on expected
overall demand for industrial finance and on the financiera's long-term
historic growth. Consequently, annual growth of its local currency approvals
(including Bank funds relent in pesos) would average about 16% throughout
1974 and about 25% in 1975-76 on the basis of a relatively low level of
approvals of Col$347.1 million in 1971. Projected foreign currency operations
are about in line with this growth. Nacional intends to increase its equity
investments substantially; an average annual growth of 26.5% throughout 1976
on the basis of Col$17.7 million in 1971 seems attainable. These projections
indicate Nacional's expectation of a continuing and increasingly high demand
for industrial finance. Unaudited 1972 results indicate that Nacional may be
able to do somewhat becter than projected.
23.       Sources of Funds.  Nacional expects substantial Bank support for
its operations the need for which is based partly on a pipeline of projects
already under consideration. Substantial resources are also expected to come
from -'F and other BR funds and through discounting operations with IFI. The
largest volume of funds, however, is projected to come from repayments, port-
folio sales and share capital increases. Given past experience, this appears



APPENDIX B
Page 7
reasonable. Reflecting a continuation of its past policy, utilization of
foreign commercial bank lines for export-import financing operations is ex-
pected to have less importance than in case of the other financieras.
Nacional is conservative in its assumptions regarding future bond placements.
Considering the success of joint bond issues in 1972, the financiera should
well exceed the expected sales of Col$149.0 million throughout 1976.
24.       Financial Projections.  As a result of the expected operations,
total assets would grow from Col$812.8 million in 1971 to Col$1,508.4 million
in 1974, at an annual growth rate of 22.7%, practically equal to the annual
growth expected throughout 1976, with growth of loan portfolio exceeding
these rates. Equity investments amounting to Col$109.5 million at end 1971
are expected to expand more slowly to Col$125.3 million in 1974 and Col$147.8
million by 1976, reflecting higher levels of new com=itments but also a
faster rotation of portfolio than in the past. Equity portfolio thus would
equal 44.3% and 39.8% of Nacional's net xorth in 1974 and 1976, respectively,
compared to 53.1%, at the end of 1971. Due to the expected substantial
annual increases of capital, Nacional's indebtedness would remain well within
its present debt/equity limitation of 5:1. However, as outlined above,
Nacional's growth assumptions are conservative and a higher debt/equity ratio
would be an incentive to raise more funds, particularly local resources. The
good quality of Nacional's portfolio and its operational record support re-
leasing its contractual debt/equity limitation to 6:1.
25.       Total reserves and retained earnings (net of dividends) are pro-
jected to rise from Col$63.5 million in 1971 to Col$99.8 million in 1974 and
Col$143.7 million in 1976. However, reserves and retained earnings are ex-_
pected to drop from 8.3% of total portfolio in 1971 to 6.5% in 1976. If the
present high quality of portfolio does not deteriorate substantially, these
proportions should allow more than adequate portfolio coverage. Throughout
the forecast period expected liquidity and debt service coverage are adequate.
Nacional's projections show that the financiera expects financial costs to
grow faster than income. Administrative costs are expected to be kept rather
low with an average of 0.8% of total assets but may well be somewhat higher
if Nacional gets increasingly involved in financing smaller clients.
26.       If projected volume of business and margins materialize, net pro-
fit would grow 20.0% annually from Col$36.9 million in 1971 to Col$91.7
million in 1976, with the return on average share capital increasing from
30.0% to 46.0%. The higher profitability is reflected in higher dividends,
expected to rise from 21.6% in 1971 to 28.8%7 in 1976, at pay-out ratios de-
clining from 75.9% to 65.6%. Due to these relatively high, but acceptable
pay-outs and the substantial capital increases expected, book value of shares
would increase only marginally, from 150.8% to 168.8% of par.
CONCLUSIONS AND RECOMMENDATIONS
27.       Operating for more than one decade, Nacional has established an
impressive record of growth, profits and sound investment decisions. The



APPENDIX B
Page 8
financiera has followed a policy aiming at a high level of profits, estab-
lishment of a good dividend record and selectivity in its clientele, geared
to finance mainly investment proposals of established and profitable
enterprises. These policies have been effective insofar as they have made
the financiera the most profitable of the Colombian financieras and the only
one whose shares are traded at the stock exchange. Under the guidance of
forceful and effective top management, Nacional has concentrated on financing
existing clients, mainly in the Department of Antioqula, many of which are
amonp the leaders of industrialization in Colombia. By strengthening these
clients, Nacional has made a substantial contribution to Colombia's economic
growth.
28.       The financiera's environment, although it has been conducive for
business, also has produced a tendency to rely excessively on studies pre-
pared by its larger customers. However, recently Nacional has made progress
in upgrading its own appraisal work. Depth of management and staffing
have not fully followed Nacional's growth. Nacional agreed to improve this
aspect of its operations, as well as its appraisal and follow-up work.
29.       The quality of both Nacional's loan and equity investment portfolios
is considerable. Its reserves position is good. So has been its financial
performance. It has made effective use of its resources. Its future pros-
pects are good. The projected debt-service capacity is sufficient. Nacional
is creditworthy and is a suitable recipient for further Bank funds.
30.       Considering the good quality of Nacional's portfolio, the finan-
ciera's ample experience and impressive operating record, it is recommended
that the debt/equity limit set in the Subsidiary Loan Agreements and the Bank
Loans be increased from its present 5:1 to 6:1 and that Nacional's Statement
of Policies and Procedures be modified accordingly. Only equity net of pro-
visions will be included in the debt/equity computation.

CORPORACION FINANCIERA DEL VALLE
INTRODUCTION
1.        Valle was established in 1961 and began operations in 1962.  The
financiera seeks primarily to further the economic development of the Cauca
Valley, one of Colombia's main agricultural areas. IFC became a shareholder
in 1969.
ORGANIZATION
2.        Share Capital and Ownership.  Valle increased its capital 15.5%
from Col$99.5 million to Col$115.0 million in 1971 and to Col$130.0 mil-
lion in 1972, largely through additional subscriptions by existing share-
holders. The composition of ownership (Annex 1) therefore showed little
change. As of end 1971, Valle was widely owqned by 187 stockholders, none
of which held more than 10% of the total; Colombian private shareholders
had 68.2%, two autonomous Government shareholders -- 6.2%, foreign and
foreign-controlled shareholders - 19.1% and IFC, 6.5%.
3.        Board of Directors and Management.  Valle's Board (Annex 2), con-
sisting of ten principals and their alternates, was elected on March 6, 1972
for a two-year period. The Board includes seven representatives of Colombian
shareholders, two of foreign shareholders and Mr. Justo Garcia-Rayneri, rep-
resenting IFC. The Board has continued to meet weekly, with only the local
members attending regularly, and to take a strong interest in the financiera's
operations.
4.        Mr. Benjamin Martinez, a former Vice President of the financiera,
has been Valle's President since 1967. His competence and dynamic personality
have been important factors in Valle's rapid growth.  He is assisted by two
able Vice Presidents.
5.        Staff and Internal Organization.  Valle has improved and strength-
ened its organization (Annex 3). The various units in its organization are
adequately staffed. Valle's internal supervision and reporting is commend-
able. Despite considerable staff turnover, Valle's Technical Department now
has a sufficient staff of capable and experienced economists and engineers.
6.        Appraisal and Follow-up.  Valle's appraisal work has reached a com-
mendable level among the financieras. Most project evaluations adequately
cover all important aspects, including economic analysis. The financiera has
quickly become familiar with the effective protection calculation, which it
introduced under the last Bank loan. Project supervision does not quite reach
the quality of appraisal work. It concentrates too much on Valle's equity
investments and problem clients and Valle has not always followed its general
policy of visiting clients twice a year. Ianagement has indicated that it



APPENDIX C
Page 2
would now upgrade and emphasize more systematic and freouent follow-up.  In
connection with evaluations of investment proposals and project supervision,
Valle often provides its clients with valuable technical assistance.
7.        Procurement and Disbursement Procedures.  Valle, like other finan-
cieras, only requires its customers to show evidence of having requested
several offers in the case of large projects involving imports of machinery
a,nd equipment. If these quotations leave any doubt or if the investment is
very substantial Valle itself may seek quotations from suppliers. For foreign
currencv disbursements, procedures and checks are thorough.  However, when
disbursements involve site visits and other forms of follow-up (as in most
local currency disbursements), although the documentation required is appro-
priate, control could be strengthened through more frequent contacts with
the client.
OPLRATTONS
8.        Lending Operations.  Loans denominated in pesos, including Bank
loans, form the main part of Valle's operations. Peso loans committed reached
Col$378.4 million in 1971, up 19% from the previous year; loan disbursements
(Annex 4) increased by over one third, from Col$254.8 million to Col$343.8
million, largely due to increasing utilization of Bank and PIF funds. Valle
has continued to concentrate its lending on enterprises located in the Cauca
Valley region, which received more than three quarters of the funds disbursed.
Industrial lending accounted for 92% of the total. Valle's lending pattern,
with 29% to food, beverages and tobacco sectors, 20% for textiles and apparel,
and almost the same percentage for metals and engineering, reflects the indus-
trial structure of the Cauca Valley. Working capital financing still con-
stituted over 50%, and loans of up to five years, 69% of total Ainancing, but
the trend towards a larger portion of fixed asset financing at longer terms
has continued. Valle's foreign exchange financing, mainly short-term export
and import financing, has only grown slowly, from US$5.8 million to US$6.0
million eqtuivalent due to increasing comnetition from commercial banks.
9.        Equity Investments and Promotions.  Valle's equity portfolio had
increased onlv marginally, from Col$31.7 million in 1970 to Col$33.6 mil-
lion in 1971 and an estimated Col$41.4 million in 1972 taking up only 18.6%
of its oTm year-end 1971 equity as against 21.1% in 1970.  The 1971 increase
in its investment portfolio was largely due to a new investment in Celanese
Colombiana and additional investments in Industria Nacional de Repuestos,
Consorcio Pesquero Colombiano and Pro-hoteles. At the same time, Valle
sold holdings in Tennessee Colombia, Siderurgica del Pacifico and Nylon de
Colombia, at an average capital gain exceeding 50% of cost.  In the recent
past, Valle has not been particularly active in equity investment and pro-
motional activities, as it has concentrated its efforts on consolidation of,
and technical assistance to existing investments. Valle intends to become
more promotion-minded in future and sees good investment prospects as a result
of the Andean Common Market. Valle is noxw studying several possible promotions



APPENDIX C
Page 3
in the food processing field, mainly for exports and in production of building
materials. It has studied and intends to implement two projects for a metal
foundry and for production of electric meters. In 1972, Valle was the leader
in establishing a savings and loan association the main purpose of which is
to support housing activities in the Cauca Valley.
10.       Other Operations.  Valle participated, together with Colombiana and
IFC, in the underwriting of a Col$8.6 million share issue of Pro-hoteles. The
financiera has continued its policy of limiting its guarantee operations;
guarantees outstanding increased only from Col$25.9 million to Col$26.6 mil-
lion in 1972.
FINANCIAL POSITION AND RESULTS
11.       Borrowings and Resource Position.  During 1971, Valle's total assets
showed a sharp increase, from Col$622.1 million to Col.$604.1 million, 1/ which
was financed as follows (in Col$ million):

The 29% increase in Valle's total assets was financed by a blend of additional
resources.  Growth of Bank funds, however, mainly due to substantial disburse-
ments under Bank Loan 625-CO, was most rapid and, apart from the credit lines
extended through BR, Bank funds have become the single most important source
of finance, accounting for 23.2% of the total in 1971, as against 16.7% a year
earlier. Valle has also received continuing support from shareholders in
the form of new share capital and has utilized substantial short-term credit
lines from foreign commercial banks for export-import financing. The relative
importance of funds from BR, from commercial banks, and own equity, however,
has continued to decline. As of end 1971, bonds, although growing at a
higher rate than total assets, still account for only 3.4% of total resources,
indicating that Valle, as well as the other financieras, will have to make
substantial efforts to increase local resource mobilization. The five
financieras initiated joint bond issues in 1972 which have been rather suc-
cessful. At the end of 1971, Valle's assured resources for financing of

Page 4
fixed assets were practically fully committed, and available local resources
were practically limited to funds from BR and cash generation from Valle's
operations.
12.       Financial Structure.  Valle's 1967-71 financial statements are given
in Annex 6. They show that Valle's overall financial structure is sound.
Despite relatively substantial increases in capital (Col$15.5 million) and
equity (Col$29.9 million) the total debt/equity ratio increased significantly
from 3.3:1 to 3.7:1 in 1971 - and to 4.0:1 if allowance is made for prudent
provisions (see para. 16) -- indicating the need for Valle to continue its
efforts to strengthen its equity base. Valle's debt/equity ratio rose to
3.9:1 in 1972 (without allowing for necessary provisions). Valle has followed
a policy of maintaining sufficient liquidity.
13.       Income, Expenses and Profitability.  The steady increase in leverage
and in volume of operations has enabled Valle to improve its profits, both
in absolute amounts, from Col$22.5 million in 1970 to Col$27.0 million and
Col$32.0 million in the following years, and relative to its paid-in capital.
Net income as percentage of average net worth declined slightly to 16.3%,
due to the considerable increase in equity in 1972. Most of Valle's income
is from lending operations; dividend income dropped from 3.6% to 3.1% of
average equity portfolio in 1971. The following shows the development of
Valle's income and expens2s as percentage of average total assets during
the last five years:
1967    1968    1969    1970    1971
Gross income                      12.3    12.3    12.6    14.8    14.8
Financial expenses                 4.1      4.8     5.5      7.0     7.8
Gross spread                       8.2      7.5     7.1      7.8      7.0
Administrative expenses            2.2      1.9     1.6      1.6      1.5
Profit before tax                  6.0      5.6     5.5      6.2      5.5
Tax                                2.3      2.2     1.9      2.0      1.6
Profit after tax                   3.7      3.4     3.6      4.0      3.9
As Valle has relied increasingly on more expensive borrowed resources, and
has increased its leverage, higher financial costs have reduce<- its overall
spread. This has been only partly counteracted by a relative decrease in
administrative costs; their level of 1.5% (1.7% in 1972) of average assets
about equals that of Colombiana, but is still far above the 1.0% for Nacional.
14.       Quality of Loan and Investment Portfolios.  Valle's loan port-
folio is basically sound. At year-end 1971, 57% was lent to companies
operating profitably, a comparatively high 35% to companies under con-
struction or in pre-operating stages, 8% to companies operating at a loss
or in technical or financial difficulties; and only one loan of Col$875,000,
to a comrany in liquidation. Total principal and interest in arrears for
more than three months, by 11 companies operating at a loss or in technical
or f-.aincial difficulties, and one in liquidation, anount to Col$9.6 million.
The total amount outstanding w-ith these clients was Col$32.7 m.illion, or
>4.5% of Valle's total loan portfolio. Valle is generally sufficiently secured.



APPENDIX C
Page 5
Managerent considers that no significant losses will be incurred in the col-
lection of loans receivable.
15.       The situation of Valle's equity portfolio (Annex 5) is somewhat less
favorable. The a-uditors qualified their opinion for 1971, since almost two
thirds of Valle's equity investments (totalling Col$33.6 million) are in com-
panies in pre-operating stages or operating at a loss. Valle expects write-
offs totalling Col$2.2 million of investments in two companies in liquidation.
Of Valle's larger exposures in companies in pre-operation or operating at a
loss, Consorcio Pesquero Colombiano is in a tight liquidity situation as a
result of substantial cost overruns, but is expected to turn around in 1972,
when the fish processing plant is scheduled to start operations. Sucroquimica
Colombiana/Sucromiles, a Valle promotion which has not yet started efficient
production due to technical difficulties, has recently been reorganized. Its
prospects are now considered good. Industria Nacional de Repuestos (IMCABE)
paid dividends in 1970, but suffered a substantial loss in 1971, when the
Govearnment permitted large imports of shock-absorbers, its main product. Con-
sequently, 121CADE's debt had to be refinanced. However, the company should
regain its profitability if future imports of its main product are kept at
a low level, as expected. Empaques del Cauca has been hampered by personnel
and markrwting problems; its prospects are unclear. Serious financial and
marketing problems of V. Burrows - Industrias El Fraile have reappeared;
results of a reorganization to reduce high production costs ,till have to
be seen. Pro--hoteles, owning an international hotel in Cali in which IFC
is a shareholder, is suffering from an insufficient cash flow in its early
stage of operations and may not become profitable before 1975.
16.       Reserves and Dividend Policy.  Valle, like the other financieras,
has no provision for portfolio losses. Reserves of Col$7.4 million for losses
on equity investments and of Col$6.7 million for doubtful debts seem suffi-
cient, given the present size and quality of Valle's portfolio, but ought to
be built up as it grows. As of end 1971, estimated Col$13.2 million 1/ of
these reserves were needed to provide for realistically estimated loss pos-
sibilities. At end 1971, the financiera's total reserves and unappropriated
surplus (net of 1971 dividends, but including legal reserves of Col$19.8
million which can only be used to absorb annual net losses) a.tounted to
Col$47.8 million, equivalent to 6.3% of total loan and equity portfolios.
17.       Valle's shares are not yet listed on either of the Colombian stock
exchanges. To increase attractiveness of its shares and to build up a divi-
dend record for possible future listing, Valle has continued its policy of
increasing dividends while making prudent allocations to reserves. Valle
declared a 16.8% dividend ouit of 1972 earnings, representing a 68.3% pay-out,
compared to 14.4% and 61.5% respectively, in 1971. Reflecting the substantial
capital increase in 1971, book value of shares increased only slightly, from
156.0% to 164.5% of par, and earnings per share, from Col$2.34 to Col$2.46.
1/   Includes the total Col$7.4 million reserve for equity investments.  Given
the satisfactory quality of Valle's loan portfolio a provision of 1% of
the total ofoutstanding loans, equivalent to Col$5.8 million can be
considered adequate for doubtful loans.



APPENDIX C

1&.       Foreign Exchange Risk.  All operations reviewed by the mission in-
dicate that the financiera is not exposed to the risk of incurring foreign
exchange losses.
19.       Audit of Accounts.  Valle is audited annually by Price Waterhouse &
Co., whose 1971 report contains a qualification regarding the realization
value of Col$21.7 million in equity investments (see para. 15).
PROSPECTS
20.       Business Forecasts.  Valle's forecasts of operations (Annex 7) assume
a 14.3% average annual growth of local currency approvals (including Bank funds
in pesos) from their already rather high Col$482.5 million level in 1971, and
an even steeper rate of growth of foreign currency approvals, through 1976.
During the forecast period, annual equity investments would rise rapidly from
a very low Col$3.1 million in 1971, to Col$27.8 million in 1976. These
projections indicate that Valle expects the demand for financing to remain
high, as it sees the volume of its business mainly determined by the avail-
ability of resources. Considering Valle's rapid growth in the past, the
expected continuing strong demand for funds and Valle's good appraisal
capacity, the financiera's projections are realistic, overall, and should be
attainable if it can obtain sufficient resources for financing of equipment
imports (mainly expected rrom the Bank). Growth in 1972 did not fully reach
projections for that year. Projected commitments of Bank funds are supported
by a well-defined pipeline of projects under consideration by Valle.
21.       Sources of Funds.  The projected increases in resources assume very
substantial Bank support, as well as fresh funds from PIF, IFF and BR. Bond
placements of ColS117 million through 1976 seem rather conservative and
Valle should substantially exceed this target. The financiera also expects
to use foreign commercial bank lines extensively for export-import financing.
The financiera intends to continue its great efforts to increase its capital
base. It was quite successful in placing Col$15.5 million new shares in 1971,
and Col$15 million in 1972. Annual increases of Col$15 million are assumed
through 1976, which seems attainable.
22.       Financial Projections.  As a result of the projected operations,
growth of total assets could level off from 41.3% in 1972, to 24.2% in 1973,
and 19.8% in 1974, compared to the 32.3% annual average growth during the last
three years. Despite the substantial capital increases expected, Valle's
indebtedness would grow faster than its equity. Consequently, the financiera
would exceed its present 5:1 debt/equity limitation in 1973, but remain below
6:1 throughout the forecast period.  To provide the necessary additional lever-
age for its operations it is recommended to release Valle's present contractual
debt/eauitv limit to 6:1. The overall good quality of Valle's portfolio, and
the financiera's experience and good record of operations argue for this
relaxation.
23.       Valle's reserves and surplus (including total annual net income)
are projected to drop from 8.8% of total portfolio in 1971, to 5.4% in 1972,



APPENDIX C
Page 7
but then to increase gradually to 7.1%°o in 1976. Valle might consider to build
up reserves faster than projected. Liquidity, although decreasing, should be
adequate through 1975, but might be somewhat tight in 1976.  Projected debt-
service coverage is sound throughout the forecast period.
24.       At the expected volume of business and financial margins, net in-
come would grow 23% annually from Col$27 million in 1971, to Col$77 million
in 1976, and rates of return on average equity would increase from 16.4%, to
24.0%. Accordingly, Valle intends to increase its dividends gradually from
14.4% of par in 1971, to a more attractive 20.4% of par in 1976, but within
the limits of prudent payout ratios not exceeding 58% of net profits. Ad-
ministrative costs are expected to decrease from 1.5% of average total assets
in 1971 to 1.3% in 1976, which, although above Macional's ratio, would be a
reasonablc cost level.
COICLUSIONS A?ID REC0IThlDJATIONS
25.       Valle has been growing quite rapidly.  Under competent and dynamic
management it has been perforning well and carrying out a useful role in fi-
nancing industrial development projects, mainly of the Cauca Valley region.
Valle's appraisal work has reached a commendable level among the financieras,
but room rem.ains to make its supervision activities more systematic and thor-
oiigh.
26.       Valle's portfolio is sound, overall.  Reserves, though sufficient,
need to be built up further as portfolio grows. Its financial prospects for
doing this are good. Valle has used Bank funds efficiently and could be ex-
pected to continue doing so. Debt-service coverage is projected to remain
sound throughout the forecast period. Valle is thus a suitable recipient of
Bank funds.
27.       In consideration of the good quality of Valle's portfolio, its
operating experience and record and its present and projected soundness of
debt-service coverage, it is recommended that the 5:1 debt/equity limit set
in the Subsidiary Loan Agreements under the Bank loans be relaxed to 6:1, and
that Valle's Statement of Policies and Procedures be modified accordingly.
Valle agreed to include necessary provisions for purposes of the debt/equity
ratio computation.

CORPORACION FINANCIERA DE CALDAS
INTRODUCTION
1.        Caldas was founded in 1961 and started operations in Manizales
the following year. It was promoted with the help of the Federacion
Nacional de Cafeteros (Federacion), the Banco Cafetero and local interests,
with the primary purpose of diversifying the economy of the region, Colom-
bia's main coffee-growing center. IFC became a shareholder in 1964.
2.        Because of financial difficulties and organizational shortcomings,
Caldas' access to the last Bank financiera loan (742-CO) was conditioned
upon satisfactory completion of requirements specified in the Subsidiary
Loan Agreement. In summary, Caldas was expected to furnish satisfactory
cash flow projections for 1971-75; borrow at least the equivalent of
US$4 million from the Federacion on satisfactory terms; protect itself
against foreign exchange risk; increase its paid-in capital by Col$20
million; improve its internal operations and control of investments; and
achieve a financial position satisfactory to the Bank. Based on the find-
ings of a mission to Caldas in July 1971, the Bank determined that Caldas
had satisfactorily met the requirements and, on September 28, 1971, ad-
vised Caldas of its eligibility to use the loan.
ORGANIZATION
3.        Share Capital and Ownership.  After a Col$19.8 million capital
increase in 1971, Caldas' paid-in share capital reached Col$116.2 million
at year-end 1971; it remained virtually unchanged in 1972. As the Federa-
cion bought almost all the new shares, it increased its position as the
largest shareholder from 21.5% to 42.4% of the shares (Annex 1). Banco
Cafetero held 11.6%, while 231 private Colombian shareholders owned another
17.2%; three Colombian official institutions, 8.5%; five foreign shareholders,
11.8%; and IFC, 8.5%.
4.        Board of Directors and Management.  Caldas' oard (see Annex 2)
has seven directors, comprising bankers, lawyers, industrialists, and re-
presentatives of the Federacion. With the reduction in the size of the board
from eleven to seven and with the institution of weekly meetings, both a part
of new policies following the Bank's advice, management has been made account-
able to the board (a former deficiency); and there is a good working re-
lationship between board and management. Dr. Luis Machado is IFC's
representative on the board.
5.        Taking over in November 1970 at a time of crisis, Mr. Eduardo
Arango has done a good job as President of Caldas, within the severe
financial and organizational limitations he has had to operate. His
personality and leadership have apparently restored confidence in Caldas



APPENDIX D
Page 2
within the local business community and on the part of the shareholders.
Management has had to dedicate most of its time to companies with opera-
tional difficulties that represent a substantial part of Caldas' invest-
ment portfolio. In addition to an administrative vice-president who works
closely with Mr. Arango, Caldas recently hired a technical vice-president
to supervise the relatively unexperienced staff. Annex 3 shows Caldas'
Organization.
6.        Staff and Internal Organization.  In April 1972, Caldas' opera-
Jional staff, consisting of the head of the credit and investment division,
two engineers and one financial analyst, was too limited in number as well
as in quality to perform appraisals and follow up adequately on Caldas'
complex portfolio.  Since then, Caldas hired two financial analysts, an
accountant and a part-time financial advisor. Caldas has agreed to strengthen
its staff to improve its appraisal and supervision capacity in connection with
the proposed loan.
7.        Appraisal and Follow-up.  As Caldas' limited staff had to con-
centrate its efforts on the larger companies in Caldas' investment portfolio
operating with difficulties, appraisal work was neglected and its appraisals
were particularly deficient in market and financial analysis. Caldas also
had difficulty in calculating the effective rate of protection. The mission
discussed with management and staff improvements of appraisal work, but
implementation required staff additions. Shortage of staff also made it
impossible to implement _ sound supervision program. The hiring of a
technical vice-president and additional staff since April 1972 have enhanced
substantially Caldas' appraisal and follow-up capacity.
8.        Procurement and Disbursement Procedures.  Caldas, like the other
financieras, relies largely on its customers for proper equipment selec-
tion. It urges them, however, to procure equipment from the most competi-
tive sources available to them and, at least for larger projects, Caldas
tries to establish whether the cost of equipment selected is reasonable.
This aspect of Caldas' operations, as well as its disbursement system, is
satisfactory.
OPERATIONS
9.        Lending Operations.  Local currency commitments (Anrex 4) de-
creased by 35%, from Col$97.5 million in 1970 to Col$63.3 nilliJn in 1971,
reflecting the more cautious attitude of the new management. Most of the
lending (70%) has, as in the past, gone to manufacturing, with chemicals
and textile/apparel receiving the largest share; the remainder (30%) went
to mining, agriculture and other sectors. In 1971, terms and the propor-
tion of fixed asset financing changed somewhat. Loans with maturities
of more than five years dropped from 59% in 1970 to 35%; while loans for
fixed assets decreased from 35% to 24%. Indications are that this tendency
has reversed in 1972. During 1971, 7% of approvals were for new projects



APPENDIX D
Page 3
as compared to 14% in 1970. The fact that the average size of Caldas' loans
has increased gradually, from Col$793 thousand in 1970, to Col$904 thousand
in 1971, and that in 1971, 63% of the lending went to the Caldas' region,
as compared to 98% in 1970, is largely due to more frequent participation of
Caldas in larger joint projects with the financieras, and projects of the
financieras channeled to Caldas to use its pre-allocation.
10.       Equity Investments and Promotions.  At year-end 1971, Caldas'
equity portfolio of 50 companies, amounting to Col$118.4 million 1/ valued
at cost was larger than Caldas' own net worth of Col$94.9 million. The
highly promotional character of Caldas' past operations has changed since
its Board adopted in April 1971 policy guidelines, complementary to its
policy statement, providing that no new project should be promoted until
Caldas' financial position has improved. Since that time Caldas has not made
any new equity investments, although during 1971 it converted Col$6.2 million
worth of loans to equity, and a further Col$5.1 million in 1972. During 1973,
partly following prior coumitments, Caldas expects to convert Col$11.8 million
worth of loans to equity. Conversion of debt to equity is justifiable in
cases of companies that show reasonable prospects of becoming profitable.
While some of the conversions resulted from prior commitments, Caldas also
converted debt to equity of marginal companies rather than liquidate them.
While it is difficult to clearly establish the prospects of each of these
companies Caldas should try to minimize these conversions, since the companies
in most trouble are highly leveraged, and have severe working capital shortages,
implying mounting needs for equity and loan funds (see paras. 13 and 25).
11.       To provide liquidity, and attempting to keep the equity portfolio
from exceeding its own net worth, during 1971 Caldas sold Col$10.3 million
worth of shares from its portfolio, and a further Col$26.0 million worth of
shares in 1972. Caldas' equity portfolio is now lower than Caldas' own equity,
taking into account the convertibility of the Pederacion's loan. (See paras.
19 and 25).
12.       Other Operations.  In addition to its regular investment opera-
tions, Caldas finances letters of credit and exports and provides guarantees
for some of its borrowers. At the end of 1971, letter of credit and export
financing outstanding amounted to Col$24.1 million and guarantees to
Col$42.6 million.
13.       Policies.  Caldas is at odds with two provisions of its policy
statement. First, Caldas' exposure in Derivados del Azufre/Quimica
Suramericana, equivalent to 27% of own equity, exceeds its 25% policy
1/ Net of minimum provisions necessary for losses on loan and equity port-
folios of Co.$18.1 million and Co.$13.3, respectively (see para. 15).



APPENDIX D
Page 4
limit. Although in the short run Caldas probably cannot reduce its ex-
posure in the company it should at least try to avoid any further increase.
Second, Caldas' shareholdings are in several cases in excess of the 25%
policy limit of the companies' total voting shares, to which Caldas would
normally limit itself. However, since these are mainly holdings in prob-
lem companies, it may be impossible for Caldas to reduce these holdings in
the short run. To adhere more closely to its additional policy guidelines
Caldas has improved its financial planning, appraisal and follow-up capacity.
INANCIAL POSITION AND RESULTS

resources changed as follows during 1971 (in Col$ million):
