Reflecting the consolidation of Caldas' operations by the new management,
total utilized resources remained practically unchanged in 1971. BR
funds, providing almost one-third of Caldas' total resources, were its
most important source of funds, followed by its own equity, which contri-
buted about one-fifth of the total, and the loan from the Federacion with
about one-sixth. In mid-1972, the Federacion's loan, originally payable
in five years, was rescheduled over ten years, including two years of
grace, free of interest charges. For the remaining term of tne loan in-
terest charges were reduced from 13.5% to 9% annually.
15.       Financial Structure.  Summarized financial statements covering
1967-71 are shown in Annex 6. Since Caldas' auditors have observed that
provisions should have been made for estimated losses on realization of
loans amounting to Col$18.1 million and equity investments of Col$13.3
ndllion, they have been incorporated in the presentation of Caldas' 1971



APPENDIX D
P age 5
financial structure. 1/ Total year-end 1971 assets stood at Col$421.9
million, practically equal to 1970. As a result of large disbursements in
1972, Caldas' total assets increased sharply to Col$546.1 million at year-
end. The debt/equity ratio (allowing for necessary provisions) was 3.9:1,
a decline from the 4.3:1 at the end of 1970 as a result of the Col$19.8
million capital increase in 1971. However, if a portion of the Federacion's
loan equivalent to the estimated provision for loans is included as "quasi-
equity" as proposed (see para. 25), the debt/equity ratio would be 2.7 at
the end of 1971 and 3.6:1 a year later. At year-end 1972, Caldas' current
ratio amounted to 1.1:1. While current assets of Caldas include substantial
past-due receivables and receivables from borrowers in serious difficulties,
indicating a weaker liquidity position than apparent from the current ratio,
the fact that the Federacion rescheduled its Col$72 million loan (including
capitalization of interests) and stopped interest charges on it, makes
Caldas' liquidity position acceptable. The proceeds of recent successful
joint financial bond issues by the five financieras have also improved Caldas'
liquidity.
16.        Income, Expenses and Profitability.  According to its auditors
Caldas' Col$3.3 million profit for 1971 was over-stated by Col$3.1 mil-
lion, which represents bad debts written off during the year against re-
serves but not charged to the profit and loss statement. Unaudited 1972
statements show a Col$5.1 million profit, which may, however, be subject
to similar qualifications. Caldas' financial performance reflects the poor
quality of its portfolio. At the end of 1971, Caldas had stopped charging
interest on loans equivalent to 12.6% of its loan portfolio and had, during
the year, rescheduled loans equivalent to 10.3% of its portfolio. These
percentages improved to 10.4% and 7.8% respectively, a year later. Loans
equivalent to 2.5% of its portfolio were converted into equity in 1971 and
1.6% in 1972. Administrative expenses as a proportion of average total assets
continued to decrease, from a high of 3.5% in 1968 to 3.1% in 1970 and
to 2.2% in 1971, and to an estimated 2.1% in 1972.
17.       Quality of Loan and Investment Portfolios.  The poor quality
of the portfolio is Caldas' main problem. At the end of 1971, as much
as 46.1% of the value of Caldas' equity and loan portfolio was invested
in companies with problematical prospects. A year later, this percentage
had decreased to 41.6%.
1/   In addition, necessary provisions of Col$11.1 million for the equity
portfolio and Col$6.1 million for the loan portfolio, estimated on
the basis of the October 31, 1971 audit report have been incorporated
in the Annexes. There was no year-end audit for 1970.

/1   Companies with poor prospects are defined as cases in which the chances
that Caldas will lose its investment are greater than those of recover-
ing it. Companies with uncertain prospects are defined as cases in
which chances of recovers are about equal to the chances cf loss.
18.       It is difficult to estimate the size and timing of the losses
Caldas may have on its portfolio. On the basis of the Col$206.6 million
of total exposure in compenies in difficulties at year-end 1971, Caldas may
have losses in the range of Col$80-115 million, to be written off in 1972-
1976.
19.       Reserves and Dividend Policy.  Caldas, like the other finan-
cieras, has no specific provisions for portfolio losses. As Caldas'
reserves and unappropriated surplus amounted only to Col$10.1 million, at
the end of 1971 and to Col$9.2 million a year later, the likely substantial
write-offs would have to be made against profits or capital. Moreover, if
Caldas is to maintain a sound financial structure, it should have the capacity,
while writing off its losses, (a) to maintain a prudent debt/e-cuity ratio
and (b) to ensure its equity does not decline below the size of i ts equity
portfolio. To determine the resources Caldas might need to reconstitute its
equity to these levels, it is necessary to take into account (a) the range
of possible losses (Col$80-115 million over 1972-76), (b) the projection of
pre-tax earnings (Col$78 million for 1972-76, see para. 24) and (c) the
timing of the losses. With regard to the last point, it is necessary to
consider that write-offs may come at a faster rate than earnings are
generated. Based on the above considerations, Caldas needs the capacity
to write off as much as Col$50-70 million in any one year. This capacity
has t'ben provided by the Federacion by making its loan of Col$72 million
convertible to equity as needed to reconstitute Caldas' equity to the levels
specified above.



APPENDIX D
Page 7
20.       Caldas last paid a dividend of 8% in 1969.  The financiera does
not expect to pay a dividend over the next five years, given the need to
retain earnings in the face of likely substantial write-offs.
21.       Audit of Accounts.  Caldas is audited annually by Price Water-
house and Company. The auditors have not expressed an opinion on the
financial statements for 1971 because Caldas has substantial and serious
under-provisions for losses on equity investments and bad debts, as
noted above.
PROSPECTS
22.       Business Forecast.  Caldas' projections (Appendix 7) 1/ assume
1972 local currency loan approvals of Col$150 million, remaining at the
high 1971 level, which resulted from Caldas' participation in several
joint Bank-financed projects together with the financieras and from
financing projects channeled to it to use its pre-allocation. On the
other hand, Caldas was slower to use the funds for its own projects.
For 1973, with less participation in joint projects likely, Caldas
expects approvals to decline to Col$115 million. Thereafter, approvals
are projected to increase by about 20% annually, which implies a real
growth rate of about 10% a year, and should correspond to industrial
growth in the Caldas region. With suitable staff for appraisal, the
rate of growth projected appears attainable. While 1972 loan approvals
were in line with projections, disbursements and, thereby, total assets in-
creased faster than expected. In the light of recent developillents, Caldas'
growth may exceed projections, overall.
23.       Sources of Funds.  Funds of about US$7 million, available at year-
end 1971, were mainly those from its pre-allocation under Bank loans. Caldas
expects to finance most of its borrower's needs for equipment imports from
Bank funds and consequently, these funds would sharply increase their import-
ance as Caldas' single largest source of funds. Great efforts, however, will
be needed to raise more than the currently projected local savings, mainly
through joint financiera bond issues. Projected use of BR funds reflects
Caldas' past access to this source. Short-term foreign exchange credit lines
available or in prospect are sufficient to carry out the forecast short-term
foreign exchange operations.
24.       Financial Projections.  If Caldas' business and resources develop
as forecast, profits before taxes and write-offs of Col$76.8 million are
projected for 1972-76. The projections are based on writing off Col$30 mil-
lion of the portfolio in 1972 and 1973. If actual write-offs are in the
Col$80-115 million range a considerable part of the projected profits may,
in fact, not materialize. On the other hand, the reduction of interest on
the Federacion's loan tends to offset this possibility. Administrative ex-
penses are projected to stay close to the 1971 figure of 2.2% of total assets.
1/   Caldas' projections are based on its 1971 statements without portfolio
loss provisions.



APPE3DIX D
Page 8
Compared to 2.1% for ilorte, which is a smaller company, and 1.0-1.7% for the
larger three financieras, Caldas' administrative expenses are reasonable.
Thiey might have to be somewhat higher than Caldas expects, given its need to
expand and improve the quality of its staff. Caldas appropriately does not
project any dividend through 1976, and plans to use profits to write off
expected losses and, if possible, to build up reserves.
25.       Total assets are expected to grow at an annual average of less than
10% throughout 1976, which may turn out to be a conservative -sumption.  With
this relatively slow assets growth, Caldas would not exceed i s 4:1 debt/equity
limitation until 1976, if the debt/equity ratio is calculated by reducing
equity for necessary portfo'lio loss provisions but including the Federacion's
loan as "quasi-equity" up to the estimated level of provisions. However, as
the other financieras, Caldas would annually estimate a reasonable level of
provisions needed.
26.       Caldas' current ratio is projected to remain about 1.1:1 throughout
1976. Thus, liquidity is expected to remain tight, particularly if Caldas has
to write-off a larger part of its portfolio than it projects, implying lower
loan and interest collections than forecast. Caldas should continue to watch
its liquidity closely and prepare annually the monthly cash flow projection
and comparisons of actual performance to the projection, as required under
its policy guidelines.
27.       Caldas' tight financial situation is also reflected in the debt-
service coverage implied by the projections. The expected higher level of
joint financiera bond issues and the rescheduling of the Federacion's loan,
however, should result in better liquidity for Caldas. After 1976, as the
worst part of Caldas' portfolio would have been liquidated, debt-service
coverage should increase.
CONCLUSIONS AND RECOMMl4ENDATIONS
23.       While Caldas has made progress in solving its problems, its recovery
is proving slower and more difficult than expected. Caldas' new management
has performed well in a difficult situation, having restored c.-ifidence in
the financiera on the part of its shareholders, creditors and local
businessmen. The recent hiring of a technical vice-president amd additional
staff is a step in the direction of strengthening staff, improving Caldas'
capt;a-',;to perform adequate project appraisals, follow-up on its investments
and improve its financial planning. The rescheduling of the loan from the
Federacion and recent joint bond issues have made Caldas' liqudity position
acceptable. It will, however, continue to be tight and will need to be
watched closely for some time to come. Quality of portfolio has shown a modest
improvement. In order to solve the problemn of expected write-offs and of
Caldas' financial structure the Federacion agreed with Caldas to convert its
Col$72 million loan into equity upon specific occurrences.



APPENDIX D
Page 9
29.       Despite the progress made under the new management, serious pro-
blems remain. In tackling the major one, the poor quality of Caldas'
portfolio, management may have been keeping some companies operating rather
than considering liquidation of companies that may ultimately not be rescued.
It will need to give particular attention to this problem.
30.       If management carries out further institutional improvements and
can clean up the portfolio and thereby further consolidate Caldas' financial
position, the financiera should be able to increase its developmental role
by helping the economic diversification of the Caldas region.
31.       Given the commitment of the Federacion to assist Caldas, the
financiera's projected satisfactory debt-service capacity and the improve-
ment in its portfolio under the new management, a modest increase in Caldas'
debt/equity limit from 4:1 to 4.5:1 is appropriate. The Federacion's loan
would be included as "quasi-equity", up to the level of estimated necessary
provisions.

APPENDIX E
Page 1
CORPORATION FINANCIERA DEL NORTE
INTRODUCTION
1.        Norte was founded in 1963 and started operations in Barranquilla in
1964. It was promoted by local interests mainly in order to foster the in-
dustrial development of Colombia's northern coastal areas. IFC became a share-
holder in 1969.
ORGANIZATION
2.        Share Capital and Ownership.  Norte's share capital increased 15.9%
to Col$56.4 million during 1972. Norte's ownership by 551 shareholders (Annex
1) continues to be the most widely spread among the financieras. As of end
1971, Colombian private shareholders owned 41.9% of the total, foreign and
foreign-controlled shareholders, 30.2% and governmental shareholders, 12.5%;
IFC had remained Norte's largest single shareholder with 15.4% of the total.
In order to assist Norte in its capital mobilization plan (para. 21), IFC
opted to take 50% of its 1971 dividend in stock (IFC/R72-41 and resolution
No. IFC 72-26).
3.        Board of Directors and Management.  Norte's Board of Directors
(Annex 2) was elected on February 29, 1972 for two years. The large number
of shareholders is reflected in the composition of the Board, which consists
of 10 principals and their alternates. The Board, which meets weekly, is
a working body. Turnover has been very small. IFC's Director resigned in
1971. Since then, Dr. Luis Machado, IFC's consultant, has been advisor to
Norte's Board.
4.        Mr. Alvaro Jaramillo has been Norte's president since inception.
He is experienced and dynamic and has greatly contributed toward the success-
ful growth of the financiera. Norte's technical vice-president, responsible
for loans and investments, died in 1972. The post has only recently been
filled with a staff member who has spent several years with the financiera.
5.        Staff and Internal Organization.  Norte's organization (Annex 3),
except for appraisal and follow-up, has kept pace with the financiera's growth.
For Norte to cope with its projected increased activities, operational staff
will have to be strengthened. There had been a higher turnover in financial
analysts than in the past and the financiera's engineer responsible for tech-
nical project evaluations was hired only early last year after his predeces-
sor's resignation. However, since then Norte has hired a second engineer and
two more economists. Furthermore, Norte agreed to employ two more economists
or engineers to strengthen its appraisal and supervision capacity for the
proposed loan.
6.        Appraisal and Follow-up.  The quality of Norte's appraisal work
still varies too widely, partly due to lack of clear guidelines for project



APPENDIX E
Page 2
evaluation and the long absence of control from the technical vice-presidency.
Focus on engineering and marketing aspects is often inadequate. Economic
analysis also will have to be strengthened further.  Norte often receives
insufficiently prepared financing proposals from its clients and must there-
fore play a substantial role in project appraisal as well as in project pre-
paration.  While this constitutes valuable technical assistance on Norte's
part, it also is a considerable strain on its staff, and Norte would do better
to limit this service to its smaller clients. Although the intensity of
Norte's follow-up visits has somewhat declined, its follow-up work is adequate.
Norte's management has been taking steps to improve the financiera's appraisal
and follow-up work.
7.        Procurement and Disbursement Procedures.  Norte, like the other
financieras, largely relies on its customers for proper equipment selection.
It urges them, however, to procure equipment from the most competitive sources
available to them and at least for large projects, Norte normally requires
that its clients present quotations from several firms of international repu-
tation. This aspect of Norte's operations, as well as its disbursement system,
is satisfactory.
OPERATIONS
8.        Lending Operations.  Peso loans, including loans for import financ-
ing with Bank funds, constitute by far the largest share of Norte's operations.
Loan approvals (Annex 4) increased by 19%, from Col$189.5 million in 1970 to
Col$225.2 million in 1971, largely due to availability of funds under the last
Banlc loan. Practically all lending (99%) has gone to industry, with paper and
printing, and chemicals receiving the largest share; the portfolio is well
diversified among industrial sectors. Loans approved in 1971 were larger than
those of the previous year (70% as against 48% of the total exceeded Col$5
million), thereby reflecting the increasing portion of generally larger than
average Bank-financed projects. Average terms and the proportion of fixed
asset financing also increased substantially, for the same reason. Loans for
working capital purposes were 27% of the total in 1970, and 18% in 1971, loans
with maturities of more than five years increased from 61% to 80% during the
period.  Loans granted to firms in Atlantico and Bolivar, Norte's natural
market, were 58%, up from 51% in 1970. The substantial portion of loans
made outside the Atlantic coast region can be largely attributed to large
Bank-financed projects in which Norte participated together with other
financieras. Norte has continued to assist its clients through export-import
and working capital financing in foreign exchange. This portion of Norte's
loan portfolio increased from US$1.6 million at the end of 1970, to US$2.3
million a year later.
9.        Equity Investments and Promotions.  Norte's equity portfolio (Annex
5) increased 22.9% in 1971. In absolute terms, however, the increase from
Col$20.1 million to Col$24.7 million (and to Col$33.1 million in 1972) was
rather snall and equity investments at the end of 1971 still took up only
34.5% of N.orte's own equity. Additional investments in two profitable companies,
Compania Pesquera Vil-ingos de Colombia (Vil-ingos), a shrimp fishing and pro-
cessing corpany, and Industrias Pecuarias y Agricolas (Ingral) involved in



APPENDIX E
Page 3
cattle raising and slaughterhouse activities, accounted for about two thirds
of the increase; the remainder were two investments in tourism projects in
Cartagena (a new hotel mainly for international tourism and a tourism pro-
motion company). All these companies were promoted by Norte. The financiera's
policy statement limits its holdings to normally 25% of the companies in which
is invests. Equity investments exceeding this limit involve two companies in
which it has not yet been feasible to reduce Norte's large original partici-
pation as promoter.  Norte has maintained a cautious attitude towards promo-
tions, although the bulk of the companies it promoted are profitable and have
good prospects.  Morte is considering several new projects which would call
for equity investments.
10.       Other Operations.  Norte has not yet carried out any underwriting
or other related operations in the capital market. Guarantees have little
importance and are only granted to well-known clients. Guarantees outstanding
dropped sharply from Col$6.8 million in 1970 to Col$2.5 million and Col$0.5
million, respectively, in 1971 and 1972.
FINANCIAL POSITON AND RFSULTS
11.       Borrowings and Resource Position.  Norte's total assets increased
rapidly from Col$269.2 million to Col$354.2 million, by 31.6%, in 1971, and even
more rapidly to Col$485.1 million, in 1972. The following shows the changes
in the composition of its resources to finance the increase in 1971 (in
Col$ million):
December 31, 1970 December 31, 1971 Increase/Decrease
% of                % of                % of
Amount      Total  Amount        Total  Amount       Total
IBRD                          73.0      27.1    129.1        36.5     56.1       66.0
Own equity                    64.9      24.1      71.5       20.2      6.6        7.8
BR lines                      64.6      24.0      68.6       19.4      4.0        4.7
Foreign commercial banks    31.0        11.5      44.8       12.7     13.8       16.2
Bonds                          7.4       2.8      12.2        3.4      4.8        5.6
Other                         28.3      10.5      28.0        7.8      (0-3)     (0.3)
Total liabilities and
equity                     269.2      100.0    354.2       100.0     85.0      100.0
IBRD funds have further increased in importance as Norte's most important single
source of finance, accounting for more than one-third of total resources. Norte
succeeded in using about one-eighth of the four Bank loans. Increase in Bank
funds accounted for two-thirds of the net increase in resources in 1971. Re-
sources provided from and through BR (except Bank funds) have only marginally
increased and have remained about equal to Norte's equity. Growth of both BR
funds and own equity, however, did not keep pace with the growth of total re-
sources. Morte continued to make efforts to place its bonds with institutional



APPENDIX E
Page 4
investors. Although bonds outstanding almost doubled in 1971, they still only
provided 3.4% of total resources at the end of that year. Norte, like the
other financieras, continues to be faced with a tight local currency situa-
tion. In 1972, however, the five financieras began a successful drive to
issue bonds jointly, and are making great efforts thereby to raise additional
local resources.
12.       Financial Structure.  Summarized financial statements covering
fiscal years 1967-1971 may be found in Annex 6. Norte's financial structure
is sound. However, due to its rapid growth its total debt/equity ratio in-
creased sharply from 3.3:1 to 4.0:1 in 1971, and 4.8:1 in 1972 (without allow-
ing for provisions), pointing to the need to increase Norte's-equity basis
in order to maintain a prudent relation to its debt. Norte's present con-
tractual debt/equity limit is 5.0:1. If allowance is made for prudent pro-
visions (see para. 16) Norte's year-end 1971 debt/equity ratio reaches 4.2:1.
Liquidity has remained satisfactory.
13.       Income, Expenses and Profitability.  Income is almost fully derived
from lending operations, with dividend income accounting for only 2.8% of total
income. Norte's profitability has been increasing steadily. Net profit in
1971 was Col$12.5 million, equivalent to a 16.1% return on average equity, up
from Col$10.0 million (14.7%) in 1971 and Col$8.2 million (13.4%) in 1970.
Norte's administrative costs of 2.1% of total assets are the highest among
the financieras, reflecting that it has the smallest total assets. However,
administrative costs declined from 2.2% to 2.1% of total assets in 1972 and,
as business expands they should decline further.
14.       Quality of Loan and Investment Portfolio.  Norte's loan portfolio
is basically sound. At the end of 1971, loans in arrears (including interest)
for more than three months totaled Col$13.0 million; the total principal out-
standing with clients in arrears was Col$21.7 million, or 7.4% of loan port-
folio. Almost two-thirds of these arrears are with companies operating pro-
fitably. With a small exception, all loans in arrears to 10 companies in
serious difficulties or in liquidation are adequately secured, and mangement
considers that no losses will be incurred in collecting receivable balances
at December 31, 1971. Ref;.nancing in 1971, all based on thorough analysis
of each refinancing proposal, involved six companies owing a total principal
of Col$18.3 million at the end of 1971. More than half of this amount was
outstanding to Caribe Inn Ltda., a luxury hotel for internatioval tourism,
the completion of which has been delayed pending the completion of the road
connecting Venezuela with Colombia's north coast. This delay is causing
substantial cost overruns. Although Norte is sufficiently secured, it is aware
that this company must be closely watched.
15.       Norte's equity portfolio (Annex 5) is quite sound overall; three-
quarters of it is invested in companies operating at a profit. Only one
company, of which Norte holds Col$0.4 million in equity, is in liquidation.
Norte expects a full write-off. Norte's largest equity investments are in



APPENDIX E
Page 5
Ingral and Vikingos (see para. 9). Both companies also received substantial
loans from Norte. Ingral is very profitable; Vikingos, which has been pro-
fitable in 1971 and 1972 apparently has passed the start-up phase of its
operations. Mr. Jaramillo dedicates ample time to both companies. Norte
also has a substantial loan and equity exposure in Consorcio Pesquero
Colombiano (see Appendix C, para. 15).
16.       Reserves and Dividend Policy.  Norte, like the other financieras, has
no specific provisions for portfolio losses. Reserves for losses on receivables
of Col$5.7 million and on equity investments of Col$1.1 million seem adequate,
given the overall good quality of Norte's portfolio. An estimated Col$1.1 mil-
lion of these reserves is only needed to provide for realistically estimated
loss possibilities; management expects no losses in the collection of re-
ceivables. At year-end 1971, the financiera's total reserves and unappropriat-
ed surplus (including a legal reserve of Col$3.4 million which may be used to
absorb annual net losses and deducting the dividend declared from 1971 earnings)
amounted to Col$16.4 million, providing a 5.1% coverage of total portfolio.
17.       Norte has continued its efforts to establish a satisfactory dividend
record to widen acceptance of its shares in the market; so far, they are not
yet registered on any stock exchange. Out of 1972 earnings, Norte declared
a 14.4% dividend, representing a 65.0% pay-out, with the option to its stock-
holders -- as in the previous year -- to accept the dividend in common shares
at par. This compares with a 13.2% dividend, at a 64.0% pay-out, for 1971. To
assist Norte in its capital mobilization efforts IFC opted to receive 50% of
its dividend for 1971 in stock. Compared to 1971, book value of shares in-
creased in 1972 from 146.9% to 148.8% of par and net income per share from
Col$2.06 to Col$2.22.
18.       Foreign Exchange Risk.  All operations reviewed by the mission indicate
that the financiera has followed its policy of not incurring the foreign exchange
risk in its operations.
19.       Audit of Accounts.  Forte is audited annually by Price Waterhouse
& Co. No qualification appears in the 1971 audit report.
PROSPECTS
20.       Business Forecasts.  Norte based its operational forecasts for
1972-1976 (for projections see Annex 7) on its expectation of a continuing
and increasing strong demand for local and foreign currency, mainly by in-
dustry.  Local currency loan approvals (including Bank ftnds in pesos) there-
fore are projected to rise to Col$349.O million in 1974, which compares to
Col$225.2 million in 1971. Projected commitments of Bank funds are partly
based on a pipeline of projects which are either under study by Norte or for
which investment proposals have been received. Projected growth of foreign
currency loans, largely import financing in connection with fixed asset loans,
even exceeds the projected growtlh of local currency lending.  New equity in-
vestments are expected to average only a modest Col$6.0 million a year, as



APPENDIX E
Page 6
against Col$11.5 million in 1971.  In view of Norte's past rapid growth and
the expected strong demand for industrial financing, these five-year projec-
tions appear somewhat optimistic but attainable, provided that Norte can
obtain sufficient resources for financing its clients' long-term foreign
exchange needs (mainly expected from the Bank) and of its clients' local
currency needs and that it can raise additional equity to increase its lend-
ing basis sufficiently. Growth of total assets in 1972 exceeded projections,
whereas net profit lagged slightly behind expectations.
21.       Sources of Funds.  As of end 1971, funds available were mainly
Col$16 million in oxn resources and unutilized short-term credit lines from
foreign commercial banks. Norte expects to finance most of its clients' needs
for equipment imports from new Bank funds, which would sharply increase in
their importance as Norte's single largest source of finance. Great efforts,
however, will be needed to raise more than the projected local savings to
match these Bank funds, mainly through joint financiera bond issues, and even
more so, to raise the projected average of about Col$10 million a year in share
capital.  Morte is making strong efforts to obtain new capital from existing
and new shareholders. Projected utilization of BR funds reflects Norte's
access to this source in the past. Short-term foreign exchange resources
available or in prospect are sufficient to carry out the forecast foreign
exchange operations.
22.       Financial Projections.  Norte's assets grew 37.2% in 1972 and are
forecast to growq about 40% each in 1973 and 1974. Given its rapid asset
growth in the past and the continuing strong demand for industrial financing,
these projections appear reasonable. By 1974, total assets would more than
double. Due to this rapid growth of assets, in spite of projected considerable
capital increases, Norte would exceed its present debt/equity limitation in
1974, and even earlier if its capital mobilization plan falls short of ex-
pectations. In order to provide the needed additional leverages for its
operations, a relaxation of Norte's debt/equity ratio is appropriate from
its present 5:1, to which it is bound by its Policy Statement and agreements
under Bankl loans, to 6:1.  The strength of Norte's portfolio and reserves
and its experience and record of operations justify the relaxation.
23.       Reserves and surplus (including total annual net incomes) are expected
to remain througb 1976 at about 7% of total portfolio, which should be adequate
if Norte continues its present prudent investrment policy. Ratios of liquidity,
a_tbougb somewhat dereasing, and debt/service coverage should be comfortable
throughout the forecast period.
24.       Assumiing that expected volume of business and financial margins are
acUlieved, Yorte's net income would more than treble from Col$fn.0 million in
1971 to Col$35.1 million in 1976, equivalent to an increase from 14.7% to 19.8%
on average equitv. Increasing profitability is also reflected in dividends
projected to rise from 13.2% in 1971 to 18.0% in 1976, at prudent pay-out levels.
Consequently, despite the projected substantial capital additions, book value



APPENDIX E
Page 7
of shares would increase from 146.8% to 192.1% of par during the five-year
period. The projected higher income is largely a result of projected higher
financial leverage and lower administrative costs, expected to fall from 2.2%
of average total assets in 1971 to 1.0% in 1976. Decreases below a level of
1.4%, however, may be difficult to achieve.
CONCLUSIONS MTD RECO1M'¶.NDATIONS
25.       Norte has been carrying out a useful and growing role in promoting
and financin,  primarily industrial projects on the Colombian North Coast,
and has become an important institution contributing to regional development.
The financiera continues to have capable management.  While its organization,
particularly its appraisal and follow-up capacity, had not fully kept pace
with the financiera's rapid growth, it has taken steps to improve these
aspects of its operations, and it agreed to take further measures designed
to enable it to handle its increased workload under the proposed loan.
26.       Norte has been performing well.  It has grown rapidly and retained
its overall good quality of portfolio and adequate reserves to cover possible
losses. It has made good use of Bank funds and is expected to continue doing
so. Projected debt/service capacity is comfortable. The financiera is credit-
worthy and is a suitable recipient for receiving Bank funds.
27.       In view of the strength of Nlorte's portfolio, adequacy of reserves,
its experience and record of operations, it is recommended that the 5:1 limit
set in the Subsidiary Loan Agreement related to the Bank loans be relaxed to
6:1, and that Norte's Policy Statement be modified accordingly. Realistically
estimated provisions would be included in this computation.

APPENDIX F
Page 1
CORPORACION FINANCIERA DE OCCIDENTE
INTRODUCTION
1.        Occidente was established in Pereira in 1966 by a group of local
businessmen, mainly to finance private manufacturing enterprises. Pereira
is the capital of Risaralda, one of Colombia's most important coffee growing
areas. Industry is still relatively unimportant in this region.
ORGANIZATION
2.        Share Capital and Ownership.  At the end of 1971, Occidente's share
capital of Col$ 31.8 million was held by 233 shareholders (Annex 1); it
remained virtually unchanged in 1972. Private Colombian nationals owned one-
half of the total. The First National City Bank group, which became a share-
holder in 1969, was the largest single shareholder group (37.7% of total),
followed by national and local coffee interests (23.7%). Occidente's shares
are not registered on any stock exchange.
3.        Board, Management and Staff.  Powers to conduct Occidente's affairs
are vested in its Board of seven directors (Annex 2), consisting in the
majority of experienced bankers and industrialists. Three members represent
the First National City Bank group, two, coffee interests and the remaining
two represent smaller shareholders. Five votes are needed for board decisions.
The board has delegated to the President authority to approve loans of up
to Col$ 50,000 and to a loan committee, composed of three board members and
the President, to approve loans up to a Col$ 2 million to one borrower, as
long as the committee votes unanimously. The board, which is elected for a
one-year period, usually meets monthly while the loan committee meets twice
a month.
4.        Mr. Gaviria, Occidente's President since inception, is capable
and dynamic. He was a director of the National Planning Department and
Technical Deputy Manager of the National Association of Industrialists.
Mr. Gaviria follows closely and is well informed about all aspects of
the financiera's operations, as is suitable in a small company. He works
closely with Mr. Vallejo, Vice-President since 1969, who gives the President
good support. Annex 3 shows Occidente's organization chart.
5.        Occidente's financial analysts responsible for equity investments,
lending for new projects and for the loan portfolio, are well qualified, but
they still lack experience. However, this is made up for by close super-
vision of management. In addition, Occidente recently hired an industrial
engineer. Staffing is sufficient for the present level of operations. The
financiera plans to hire a second engineer as well as a financial analyst
in the near future.



APPENDIX F
Page 2
6.        Appraisal and Follow-up.  Although Occidente has lent mostly for
short-term working capital, its staff has experience in preparing appraisals
for fixed asset financing with PIF funds and for a few projects it intended
to finance with Bank funds through two of the five financieras. Overall,
these appraisals are satisfactory, although they show weaknesses in financial
analysis, particularly in analyzing debt service capacity of applicants.
With continuing experience, Occidente's appraisals should improve substantially.
7.        Occidente follows up closely on its investments, except for those
located in the Bogota region, which are visited more infrequently.  Occidente
intends to close this gap with the hiring of the abovementioned financial
analyst. Follow-up reports are generally complete and clearly describe the
situations of investments; however, they show the same weakness as the
appraisal reports. Occidente regularly informs its borrowers of results of
follow-up visits and makes specific recommendations whenever necessary.
8.        Procurement and Disbursement Procedures.  Given Occidente's present
level of operations, procedures are satisfactory overall. It generally checks
on capital costs and procurement aspects of projects and on adequacy of
construction cost.
OPERATIONS
9.        Lending Operations.  In 1971, the sixth year of operation, Occidente's
loan disbursements amounted to Col$ 102.9 million, sharply up from Col$ 71.0
million in 1970 and Col$ 42.2 million in 1969 (Annex 4). Due to the rapid
growth in disbursements, its loan portfolio rose more than 50% in 1970, from
Col$ 39.2 million to Col$ 61.0 million, followed by a 40% increase to Col$ 85.4
million in 1971.
10.       Over the last three years, Occidente has made predominantly small,
short-term loans to existing enterprises for financing working capital.
However, a clear trend in the opposite direction is visible. Between 1969-71,
the average loan size was Col$ 302,000, with 53% of loans disbursed under
Col$ 500,000, 68% were loans for up to two years; 29%, for two to five years;
and only 4%, for more than five years. As much as 82% of loan disbursements
were for working capital, and 97% for existing companies. The industrial
sector received 92% of the total disbursed, with the bulk (29%) for textile
industries. Although 55% of loans were made in Risaralda, as much as 30%
was for the Bogota region, which has shown a tendency to receive an increasing
share.
11.       Equity Investments.  Occidente's portfolio contains 13 equity
investments amounting to Col$ 16.5 million, equivalent to 14% of its total
loan and equity portfolio and to 47% of its own equity (Annex 5).  Five of
the companies have been promoted by Occidente. The major part of the portfolio,
Col$ 9.2 million, is in the shares of Comestibles La Rosa, Colombia's largest
food processer, a subsidiary of the Nestle Company.



APPENDIX F
Page 3
12.       Other Operations.  In addition to its regular lending operations,
Occidente finances letters of credit and exports, and provides guarantees.
At the end of 1971, Col$ 17.8 million in letters of credit and export financing
was outstanding, and Col$ 9.7 million in guarantees.
13.       Policies.  In April 1972, Occidente's board approved a Policy
Statement (Annex 8), resembling those of the five financieras, which it
intends to adhere to, and which contains all the provisions usual to develop-
ment finance companies associated with the Bank group. At present, it owns
more than the 25% of the voting shares of six companies to which it would
limit itself normally. Reductions of its exposure in these companies will
only be possible in the long run as these investments -- most of which have
been promoted by Occidente -- mature.
FINANCIAL POSITION AND RESULTS
14.       Resources.  Occidente has financed its operations largely from its
own capital. During the 1969-1971 period, 52% of disbursements had been
financed with its own equity. Its next most important source of funds is the
five year bonds it issues; at the end of 1971, Col$ 26.9 million were out-
standing out of a total of Col$ 43 million issued. The only long-term borrow-
ings available to Occidente are PIF funds, of which Col$ 8.2 million were
utilized at the end of 1971. In short-term funds, it had time deposits of
Col$ 11.1 million, and it had drawn US$ 842,000 of its US$ 1.2 million credit
lines from foreign commercial banks. For long-term investment, Occidente has
only PIF funds and its own equity available.
15.       Financial Structure.  Financial statements for the years 1967-1971
are shown in Annex 6. Total assets at the end of 1972 stood at Col$191.7
million, representing a 52% increase over 1971 (the increase had been 63%
to Col$126.2 million in 1971), reflecting Occidente's rapidly growing opera-
tions. Its financial structure at year-end 1971 was sound with a 2.9:1
total debt to equity ratio and a current ratio of 1.3:1. Indebtedness had
increased to 3.7 times debt by the end of 1972.
16.       Income, Expenses and Profitability.  Since it started operating in
1967, Occidente has developed a good profit record. In 1972, net profit after
taxes was Col$6.3 million, up 75% from Col$3.6 million in 1971. The table
below shows the development of income and expenditures for 1971, expressed
as percentages of average total assets:
1968      1969       1970       1971
Total income               13.1      13.2       13.1       14.3
Financial expenses          3.9       5.1        4.2        6.0
Gross spread                9.2       8.1        8.9        8.3
Administrative expenses   3.4         3.1        2.8        2.9
Profit before tax           5.8       5.0        6.1        5.4
Tax                         1.5       1.5        2.1        1.7
Net Profit            4.3        3.5        4.0        3.7



APPENDIX F
Page 4
Higher gross income in 1971 stemmed largely from an increase in guaranty and
letter of credit business, mainly through its connection with the First National
City Bank. The drop in financial expenses in 1970 reflects the large share
capital increase from Col$ 13.8 million to Col$ 29.0 million during that year,
while the increase in 1971 is a result of the increased indebtedness, as the
debt/equity ratio rose from 1.3:1 to 2.9:1. The result was a decline in the
spread in 1971, which led to a reduction in net profit as a percentage of
average total assets. Despite the decrease in net profit on average total
assets, as a result of increased financial leverage profit after taxes on
year-end share capital increased from 8.6% in 1970, to 12.2% in 1971. The
return on share capital is still somewhat low, reflecting relatively high
financial and, due to Occidente's small size, administrative costs. The
latter, amounting to 2.9% of average total assets in 1971, are likely to
decrease with the projected growth of the company. Administrative costs
dropped sharply to 2.4% of average total assets in 1972, due to the rapid
increase in operations.
17.       Quality of Loan and Investment Portfolio.  Occidente's portfolio
is of good quality, overall. At the end of 1971, Occidente's outstanding
loans in arrears -- all with companies whose prospects were poor or uncertain -
amounted to Col$ 4.7 million, equivalent to 5% of the loan portfolio. With
the exception of a Col$ 2.7 million loan to a radio producer, these loans are
well secured, as is most of Occidente's portfolio. Consequently, losses on the
loan portfolio are not expected to be large. Of loans to companies in financial
difficulty, only the above-mentioned loan was rescheduled.
18.       Only Col$ 1.6 million, or one tenth of Occidente's equity portfolio,
is in companies with uncertain prospects: Col$ 400,000 with the radio manu-
facturer, Col$ 1.0 million in three promotional companies, and Col$ 200,000
in a company in liquidation expected to be a write-off.
19.       Reserves and Dividend Policy. Like the other financieras, Occidente
has no provisions for portfolio losses. It has reserves for bad debts of
Col$ 189,000 and for losses on equity investment of Col$ 200,000. Its total
reserves and unappropriated surplus (including a legal reserve of Col$ 1.6
million, which can be used only if Occidente shows operating losses but
excluding dividends for 1971) amounted to Col$ 2.9 million (net of 1971
dividend), equivalent to 2.2% of total portfolio at the end of 1971. Even
considering the good quality of Occidente's portfolio, reserves need to be
strengthened.
20.       Occidente started paying dividends in stock, amounting to 7% and
10%, respectively, in 1968 and 1969. After the entry of the First National
City Bank group as shareholder, Occidente paid cash dividends of 7.7% in
1970, resulting in a 76% pay-out, and of 10% in 1971, equivalent to an 83%
pay-out. These pay-out ratios are high, given the need for Occidente to
build up its reserves particularly in the light of its rapidly expanding
operations. Occidente maintained the 10% dividend on its 1972 earnings,
resulting in a substantial reduction in pay-out to 61%. As a result of
the high dividend pay-out and increases in share capital, book value of



APPENDIX F
Page 5
shares was only 121% of par at the end of 1971, approximately the same as
three years earlier.
21.       Foreign Exchange Risk.  All operations reviewed by the joint mission
indicate that Occidente protects itself adequately in its operations against
foreign exchange risk.
22.       Audit of Accounts.  Since 1969, Occidente has been audited annually
by Price Waterhouse & Co., whose report for 1971 contains no qualifications.
PROSPECTS
23.       Occidente's Business Forecast.  Occidente projects (Annex 7) that
loan approvals will increase at an average of 12% a year from Col$ 165.4
million in 1971 to Col$ 290 million in 1976, and that new equity investments
of Col$ 2-3 million a year will be made. This moderate growth rate should
be easily attainable, given the rapid growth of the company in the last
few years and its good management, if matched by a similar growth of equity.
Results for 1972 virtually coincide with projections.
24.       Resource Needs.  Total commitments during the five year period are
expected to be over' one billion pesos, mostly to be financed with Occidente's
own funds, bond issues, PIF and foreign bank credit lines. Partly based on
a project pipeline, Occidente forecasts a need for commitments of Bank funds
equivalent to Col$ 117.5 million during 1973-76. The need for World Bank
resources to meet 1973 and 1974 commitments would be about US$ 1.8 million.
25.       Financial Projections.  If Occidente's business and resources
develop as forecast, annual growth of total assets would average 28% through-
out the forecast period, while net profit after taxes is projected to in-
crease from Col$ 3.6 million in 1971, to Col$ 16.5 million in 1976. This
growth would be slower than the actual growth over the last five years.
Assuming share capital increases of Col$ 2.7 million in 1972, Col$ 6.2 million
in 1973, Col$ 7 million in 1974, and Col$ 5 million in 1975, which Occidente
expects to raise mainly from existing shareholders, profit after taxes on
year-end share capital would increase from 12.2% to 33.1% in 1976, while
profit before taxes on average net worth would rise from 15.5% in 1971, to
34.2% in 1976. Administrative expenses as a percentage of average total
assets would decline from 2.9% in 1971, tto 1.8% in 1972 and reach a rather
low 1.0% at the end of 1976, about equal to present expenses of the largest
financieras.
26.       Dividends of 16% on par value are projected for 1972, increasing
to 27% in 1976, implying extremely high pay-out ratios, ranging from 81%
to 86%. With these sizeable dividends, reserves and surplus (excluding
projected dividends) would increase from Col$ 3.2 million in 1971 to
Col$ 4.3 million in 1972, and reach Col$ 11.0 million in 1976, while their
relationship to the total loan and equity investment portfolio would drop



APPENDIX F
Page 6
from 2.3% in 1971 to 2.2% in 1972 and reach only 2.5% by 1976. In view of
its rapidly expanding operations and growing portfolio of equity investments
and long-term loans, Occidente should have larger reserves. For this purpose,
its pay-out ratio should not exceed about 60% of earnings. Dividend pay-out
in 1972 amounted to 61% of earning showing Occidente's increasing awareness
for building up its reserves.
27.       Occidente's debt to equity ratio reached 3.7:1 at the end of 1972
and is projected to increase to 5.0:1 at the end of 1976, which indicates
that Occidente will either have to raise more capital or reduce its dividend
pay-out. If the pay-out ratio were limited to 50%, Occidente would not
exceed the 4:1 debt to equity considered reasonable for it, until 1974. BR
Intends to ensure that Occidente follows in future a prudent dividend and
reserve policy as stated in its Policy Statementp
28.       Occidente's debt service coverage, as implied by its projections,
would be satisfactory.  During the projected period, collections on loans
are expected to amount to a minimum of 1.2 times the amounts of repayments
on debts. Profits before financial expenses will be more than 1.7 times
interest payable.
CONCLUSIONS AND RECOMMENDATIONS
29.       Conclusions.  Although Occidente has engaged predominantly in
extending short-term working capital loans, it has also financed a number of
fixed asset investments and has a sizeable equity investment portfolio.
Past growth of operations has been impressive. Occidente's management is
dynamic and capable. Its staff has adequate capability for project appraisal,
follow-up and technical assistance to clients. Operating procedures are
generally satisfactory, although procurement practices leave room for improve-
ment. Occidente's financial situation is good. Its loan and equity portfolio
is of good quality. Loans generally have adequate security coverage. How-
ever, Occidente needs to continue making strong efforts to increase its re-
serves. With reasonable administrative costs it has achieved an acceptable
level of profitability. Prospects for future growth of operations are good.
30.       Recommendations.  It is recommended to accept BR's proposal that
Occidente participate in the proposed loan as it is a suitable recipient of
Bank funds.

COKPORACION FINANCIEXA DE OCCIDENTE
Statement of Policies and Procedures
1.     The Corporacion Financiera de Occidente shall direst its
efforts toward financing the Colombian private sector,
giving special attention to the manufacturing industry,
and its programs shall follow the general development guide-
lines approved by the National Government.
2.     The (rporacion shall seek to give priority to the follow-
ing activities:
(a)    Promotion of the organization and reorganization of
manufacturing,agricultural, mining and tourism enter-
prises which, after the necessary feasibility studies,
will seek to ensure both Colombia's economic develop-
ment and a reasonable return on investment.
(b)    Participation in the capital of enterprises promoted
directly by it or in those in the process of expansion.
(c)    The granting of credit at terms not exceeding 20 years
to manufacturing, agricultural, mining, transport, tourism
and other enterprises approved by Colombian legislation,
with the exception of activities involving housing and
real estate. The Corporacion shall supervise every credit
operation from the time of the loan application throughout
the life of the credit, and shall engage the necessary
qualified personnel to perform this task.
(d)    Stimulation of Colombian exports by using available domestic
lines of credit or by seeking foreign financing.
3.     The Corporaci6n shall make every effort to promote the development
of the Colombian capital market as follows:
(a)    By involving foreign capital in projects that it considers
sound, in the interests of the country's development and
likely to promote the growth of exports.
(b)    It shall seek lines of credit abroad to supplement its
available resources, in order to use such funds for the
purpos6 described in the Corporacion's general credit
policy.
(c)   It shall act as an intermediary in the placing of shares,
bonds and other securities issued by private Colombian enter-
prises, with a view to channeling and orienting private savings
into economically productive activities.



APPENDIX F
Anne-x -
COLOMBIA           Page 2
FIFTH FINANCIERA PROJECT
CORPORACION FINANCIERA DE OCCIDENTE
(d)    It shall strive to expand the issues and placements of its
own bonds.
4.   It shall seek to orient its financial policy in such a way that it
can be an effective agent of economic development. To that end, it shall
operate in accordance with the following rules:
(a)    The Corporacion shall allocate a steadily increasing proportion
of its resources for capital investments, subject to the limitations
set forth in point 7, and for medium- and long-term credits.
(b)    The total amount of loans, capital investments and -guarantees extended
by the Corporacion to any company shall not normally exceed 15% of
the Corporacion' s net worth (patrimonio neto). iceptions to this rule
shall only be made following a thorough examination of the case by
the Board of Directors of the Corporacion and shall be restricted
to companies that have sound financial prospects but do not have the
capacity to secure sufficient capital elsewhere. In no event shall
the total amount of such loans, investments and guarantees exceed
25% of the Corporacion's net worth.
(c)   The Corporaci6n shall pursue a prudent dividend policy that will
enable it to bLild up adequate reserves, at the discretion of its
Board of Directors, while at the same time distributing a proper
return to its stockholders.
(d)   The Corporacion shall not contract, assume or guarantee any debt
if, at the same time, or as a result of such operation, the total
indebtedness contracted, assumed or guaranteed by the Corporacion,
and still outstanding, exceeds 4 times its net worth.
(e)   The maturities of the Corporaci6n's assets shall be equal to or
shorter than those of its liabilities.
(f)   The minimum amount of any credit transaction between the Corpora-
cion and any one company shall be one hundred thousand Colomtian
pesos ($100,000), save in exceptional circumstances.
5.    (a)   The Corporaci6n shall maintain a qualified staff in its Appraisal
and Supervision Departments to ensure the efficiency of its credit
operations and the security of its investments.
(b)   To the extent possible, the Corporacion's staff shall provide its
customers with technical assistance, so that when the latter prepare
studies or projects they can be sure of proper advisory services either
from the Corporaci6n itself or from other institutions or competent
persons recommended by the Corporaci6n.
6.    The Corporaci6n's participation in a company's capital shall not normally
exceed 25% of paid-up capital, save in exceptional circumstances or situa-
tions of a tempamry nature when the Corporaci6n's participation may exceed
this percentage.



APPENDIX F
Annex 8
Page 3
COLOBIA
FIFTH FINANCIERA PROJECT
CORPORACION FINANCIERA DE OCCIDENTE
7.     The total capital investments of the Corporacion shall not exceed
its net worth.
S.     The Corporacion shall seek to protect itself adequately against any
exchange risks that may arise from its operations.
9.     The Corporacion shall hire independent public accountants to conduct
an annual audit of its books and accounts.
10.    Any decision on a matter in which any one of the directors has an
interest shall be taken solely by the other directors. The director
involved shall take no part either in the discussion or in the voting.
11.    The management of the Corporacion shall submit to the Board monthly
reports including:
(a)  Progress of investments made;
(b) Investigation of new projects;
(c) Financial statements;
(d) Overdue items in portfolio;
(e)  Other matters having a major bearing on the Corporacion's
financial position and operations.

APPENDIX G
Page 1
CORPORACION FINANCIERA DEL CARIBE
INTRODUCTION
1.        Set up by a group of Dutch businessmen and the Banco de Colombia,
by which it was managed until the end of 1969, the Corporacion Financiera
del Caribe started operations in Bogota in 1968. The Bogota area, the cen-
ter of Caribe's operations, is the most highly industrialized of the coun-
try, accounting for 27.1% of total value added in 1967, and 30.2%"o of employ-
ment in manufacturing.
ORGANIZATION
2.        Share Capital and Ownership.  At the end of 1969, the Algemene
Bank rroup (Algemene Bank Nederland N.V., Hollandsche Bank Unie, N.V. and
Inter-American Trade and Development Corporation, N.V.) bought all of Caribe's
shares, with the intention of using it as the nucleus of a financial group
in Colombia; also to include a commercial bank, an insurance company and a
sales financing company. As the Algenene Bank group (Algemene) wanted to
maintain the majority, it sought shareholders willing to take ca minority
position. Subsequently, the Colombian Caracol group, which operates the
country's largest radio and television network, and three foreign enter-
prises entered as minority shareholders. Thus, at year-end 1971, foreign
interests held 75% of Caribe's Col$ 20 million paid-in share capital, with
Algemene owning 55%, and the Caracol group, 25% (Annex 1). Paid-in capital
increased slitghtly, to Col$ 20.5 million, in 1972.
3.        Board, Management and Staff.  Caribe's board (Annex 2) of six di-
rectors, three of which represent Algemene and three Caracol, comprises some
leading Colombian bankers and businessmen. Tne board, elected for a two-year
period, usually meets once a month with all members attending regularly.
Chairmanship is rotated annually between representatives of Algemene and
Caracol. The board has delegated authority to a management loan committee
to approve loans (a) up to Col$ 1.2 million without mortgages or liens and
up to Col$ 3 million with such security, if one board member participates
and (b) up to Col$ 1.8 million, and Col$ 4.5 million, respectively, if two
board members participate. Board members representing Algemene must obtain
prior approval from Amsterdam for loans exceeding these amounts and for all
equity investments.
4.        In 1970-71, Caribe was managed by a Colombian President and a Dutch
Vice-President, supplied by Algemene. Both left Caribe by the end of 1971.
Caribe's new organization (see Annex 3) provides for one President and two
Vice-Presidents. At present, however, only the Commercial Vice Presidency
is filled, by Mr. Valencia who joined the company in January 1972 and is
acting president. Mr. Valencia had been on the staff of IFI and Caldas
and was most recently Caldas' technical vice-president. Caribe's share-
holders apparently intend to nominate a distinguished Colombian of national
stature as President, who would mainly be responsible for major policy issues



APPENDIX G
Page 2
and government and public relations, but would not directly intervene in
Caribe's operations. His main task vould be to take a leading role in form-
ing the financial group envisaged by Algemene. A Dutch employee of Algemene
is to be nominated as administrative vice-president. At present, management
is inadequate considering, in particular, that Caribe has a relatively weak
staff.
5.        Caribe's small professional staff reflects the company's past
policy of relying on consultants for project appraisal and follow-up work.
The engineer, on whom Caribe was to mainly rely for project appraisal and
follow-up, apparently left the company in October after a seven-month stay,
to return to Caldas, from where he had been hired. The compan'v secretary,
who set up the Industrial Participation Certificate system (para. 15) and
reviewed some credit operations, left in May 1972. Other professionals in-
clude an accountant and two staff members with commercial banking experience,
in charge of the treasury and foreign exchange sections. In view of the
limited experience of Caribe's small staff, its performance will depend on
the quality of the supervision it receives from management.
6.        Appraisal and Follow-up.  Caribe has done almost entirely commer-
cial bank type lending, namely short-term working capital loans based mainly
on the board's knowledge of its borrowers, or on the security provided. Its
experience in long-term project appraisal is thus very limited. For its few
loans for fixed assets, Caribe used consultants to investigate technical as-
pects of projects. The market and financial aspects were mostly not appraised
specifically; instead, Caribe relied on the borrower's general reputation.
7.        Caribe's supervision of borrowers has been irregular; recent fi-
nancial information on them is scarce. Caribe's main concern has been its
borrowers' repayment records and the collateral business (letters of credit)
they offer, but it has hardly provided any technical assistance. In case
of projects in difficulties, consultants were used for follow-up, with the
same shortcomings as mentioned in the previous paragraph. Since Caribe's
staff has changed almost entirely, its past appraisal and supervision record
cannot be used to evaluate its present capacity in these fields.
8.        Procurement and Disbursement Procedures.  At present, Caribe ex-
ercises practically no control over its borrowers' procurement practices.
Moreover, disbursement procedures are also inadequate, with chacks limited
to the adequacy of the guarantees. The acting president is preparing an
operating manual outlining appraisal, follow-up, procurement and disbursement
procedures, which he intends to introduce.
OPERATIONS
9.        Lending Operations.  A breakdown of peso lending operations is
given in Annex 4. In 1971, its fourth year of operations, Caribe committed
loans totaling Col$ 31.7 million, compared to Col$ 14.2 million in 1970, and
Col$ 20.8 million in 1969. As Caribe financed mainly short-term working cap-
ital, time lags between commitments and disbursements were minimal, and both



APPENDIX G
Page 3
figures were equal for each year. The fast growth in commitments in 1971
reflects Caribe's more dynamic approach to business after the entry of the
new shareholders. As a result, total loans outstanding grew 63% in 1971,
from Col$ 27.7 million to Col$ 45.1 million.
10.       Caribe's lending has been characterized by a predominance of short-
term loans for financing working capital. Since inception, 75% of its loans
were for one to two years, 21%, two to five years, and only 4% (two loans
made in 1969) for more than five years; 38% of loans have been for working
capital, the remainder for fixed assets.
11.       Reflecting Caribe's generally cautious attitude, 97% of total
loans were committed for existing enterprises and only 3%/ for new ones.  A
breakdown by sectors shows that 54% of Caribe's loan commitments were for
manufacturing industries, and 10% for agriculture. A high proportion (35%)
has been committed for financing building construction. Caribe's lending
was concentrated in the Bogota area, which received 67%7 of loan commitments.
12.       Equity Investments.  Caribe has made only five equity investments
(Annex 5), totaling Col$ 1.4 million, the largest of which, amounting to Col$ 1.1
million, was promoted by the financiera. At the end of 1971, its equity port-
folio represented only 3% of its total loan and equity investments, and only
5.5Z of its own equity. Caribe is not presently considering any promotions.
13.       Other Operations.  Through 1971, Caribe did not extend guarantees.
It has, however, provided letter of credit and export financing.
14.       The most important of its non-lending activities, issuing Industrial
Participation Certificates (certificado de participacion) is a commercial paper
operation up to now only with the Colombian subsidiary of Phillips N.V., and
represents an innovation in the Colombian capital market. Phillips issues
six-month promissory notes to Caribe, on the basis of which Caribe issues a
Participation Certificate which states that the buyer is participating in paper
held in trust by Caribe. As each certificate is matched by a Phillips promis-
sory note, Caribe acts only as a trustee, and does not guarantee the paper.
Caribe's contract with Phillips provides for issuance of Col$ 50 million in
certificates. At the end of 1972, Col$ 11.7 million was outstanding, most
of it sold directly by Caribe and about one tenth through the stock exchange.
The bulk was taken by institutional investors.
15.       Policies.  Caribe's board has approved the Policy Statement shown
in Annex 8. It is similar to the Policy Statements of the five financieras.
IJith the exception of the 20:1 debt/equity limit (para. 30), it contains all
the provisions generally usual to development finance companies connected with
the Bank Group. "esults of past operations are in line with the recently adopted
policy except for Caribe's 381% holding of Astilleros del Pacifico shares, which
is over the 25% limit of its Policy Statement, which it would normally adhere
to. As the company is new and has uncertain prospects, Caribe may not be able
to reduce its holding to 25% for some time.



APPENDIX G
Page 4
FINANCIAL POSITION AND RESULTS
16.       Resources.  Up to 1971, Caribe has been financing its operations
mainly from its own capital; this has accounted for 74% of commitments since
its inception. All of Caribe's term debt stems from BR funds,-mainly for
medium-term working capital financing. At the end of 1971, Caribe had
Col$ 17.6 million outstanding for working capital from BR (decree 2369/60
and IFF) and Col$ 3.7 million in PIF funds. It has not yet raised any funds
through bond issues. Of the US$ 1.0 million equivalent short-term foreign
exchange credit line available from Algemiene, only US$80,000 was utilized.
Other than its o,n equity and funds available from PIP, Caribe has no long-
term resources available.
17.       Financial Structure.  Unaudited income statements and balance
sheets for the years 1968-71 are shown in Annex 6. Total assets at the end
of 197? stood at Col$ 80.7 million, representing a 62% increase over 1971,
and reflecting Caribe's growing operations since the entry of the new share-
liolders.  The increase had been 52% in 1971.  Since Caribe had relied mainly
on its own resources, its total debt to equity ratio amounted to only 0.9:1
at the end of 1971. I-1owever, with a sharp increase in borrowing in 1972
this ratio rose to 1.7,' at year end.  At the same time, Caribe's current
ratio dropped From 3.3:1 to 2.2:1. Caribe's extremely low leverage and
strong liquidity position show its past highly conservative approach to
business.
18.       Income, Expenses and Profitability.  In 1972, Caribe's net profit
after taxes was Col$ 3.6 million, up 57% from Col$ 1.9 million in 1971, i.e.
sli,htly less than the 62% increase of its assets. The table below shows
the development of income and expenditures, expressed as percentages of
average total assets:
1969    1970    1971
Gross income                          14.0    15.6    17.2
Financial expenses                     3.7     3.5      3.4
Gross spread                          10.3    12.1    13.8
Administrative expenses                1.3      2.9     5.4
Profit before tax                      9.0     9.2      8.4
Tax                                    2.7     2.9      2.9
Net profit                             6.3     6.3      5.5
The percentage increase in gross income stems mainly from Caribe's signifi-
cant increase in financing letters of credit of its clients. Despite higher
gross income, net profit relative to average total assets declined in 1971,
due to the large increase in administrative expenses, from 2.9% of average
total assets in 1970 to 5.4% in 1971 an(1 in 1972. These very high admin-
istrative expenses reflect a costly management structure.



APPENDIX G
Page 5
19.       With the lower profit on average total assets, and almost unchanged
low financial leverage, profit before taxes on average net worth decreased
from 16.1% in 1970, to 15.9% in 1971, and profit after taxes on year-end
share capital from 12.7% to 11.5%.  These figures are still relatively high,
considering Caribe's low financial leverage and high administrative costs.
20.       Quality of Loan and Investment Portfolio.  Caribe's portfolio is
of good quality and generally well diversified without undue risk concentra-
tion in individual companies. At the end of 1971, no loans were in arrears
for over three months. In 1971, loans to three companies undergoing some
difficulties were rescheduled. Their total principal outstanding was
Col$ 5.3 million, or 12%' of Caribe's year-end portfolio. These companies
were the only ones in Caribe's portfolio with uncretain prospects. Except
for one of these companies, where Col$ 900,000 was not covered by guarantees,
the loan portfolio is well secured and no losses are expected at present.
21.       Caribe's small equity portfolio (Annex 5) of Col$ 1.4 million at
the end of 1971, included investments of Col$ 200,000 in two companies in
liquidation, on which Caribe expects to lose Col$ 150,000. Caribe's largest
equity investment of Col$ 1.1 million, is in a shipyard promoted by the fi-
nanciera. It began operations in 1972. Its prospects are still uncertain,
and the company apparently has management difficulties. However, through
its director on the board, Caribe is following the company's situation
closely.
22.       Reserves and Dividend Policy.  Caribe has no specific reserve for
bad debts, nor provisions for portfolio losses. Its reserves (including
legal reserves of Col$ 2.5 million only to be used if Caribe shows operating
losses), and unappropriated surplus, were Col$ 5.6 million at the end of 1971,
equivalent to 12.5% of Caribe's total portfolio. This is more than adequate
given the generally good quality of its portfolio and the sufficient security
for its loans. In future, Caribe would be well advised to make specific
allocations to portfolio reserves.
23.       In 1968 and 1969, Caribe paid a 4% dividend with pay-outs amounting
to 70% and 41% respectively. Since then, after Algemene gained control, no
dividends have been paid.  A 5% divicdend was derlared from 1972 earnings.
With the complete reinvestment of earnings up to 1971, book value increased
rapidly to 128"o of par in 1971, in spite of a Col$ 5.0 million capital in-
crease during that year.
24.       Foreign Exchange Risk Average.  All operations reviewed by the joint
mission indicate that in its operations, Caribe is not exposed to any foreign
exchange risk.
25.       Audit of Accounts.  In the past, Caribe's accounts were not audited
independently. Following the provision of its new Policy Statement, Caribe
is to be audited annually. It intends to use the auditors who audit Algemene,
Klynveldt and Kraayenhof, a Dutch firm with extensive international operations.



APPENDIX G
Page 6
PROSPECTS
26.       Caribe's Business Forecasts.  Caribe projects (Annex 7) loan ap-
provals to increase from its Col$ 31.7 million level in 1971 by 50% in 1972,
40% in 1973, 35% in 1974 and 25% each in 1975-76.  With suitable management
and staff, these growth rates appear attainable, particularly in view of ac-
tual growth in 1971 and Caribe's now more dynamic orientation.  Terms of loan
approvals would change significantly: loans approved for one year would drop
from 66%"o of the total in 1972, to only 40% in 1976. Caribe's projections
foresee no equity investments, indicating that it intends to keep its cautious
attitude in this respect.  Growth of assets and profits in 1972 was substan-
tially higher than projected.
27.       Resource Needs.  Caribe's projections imply a yearly increase of
25-38% in funds from IFF and PIF, and assume bond placements of Col$ 6.0
rmillion in 1973, Col$ 9.0 million in 1974, Col$ 11.2 million in 1975, and
Col$ 19.5 million in 1976. Caribe gives its need for World Bank resources
at ColS 111.7 million during the forecast period of wqhich it expects to com-
mit about US$1.5 million equivalent in 1973-74, mainly for a sharp increase
in its business for small and medium-sized enterprises.
28.       Financial Projections.  If Caribe's business and resource forecasts
materialize, total assets wrould grow 30.4% annually, and net profit after tax,
from Col$ 2.3 million in 1971, to Col$ 3.1 million in 1972, and to Col$ 5.0
million in 1976. Assuming share capital increases by existing shareholders
at Col$ 5 million each in 1974 and 1975, profits after taxes on average paid-
in share capital would rise from 13.1% in 1971, to 15.5%' in 1972, and reach
16.7% in 1976, and profit before taxes on average equity would rise from 15.9%
in 1971, to 18.8% in 1972, but decline to 16.2%o in 1976, as a result of the
large retention of earnings and share capital increases. Throughout the
period, profitability due to low leverage and relatively high administra-
tive expenses remains modest. Administrative expenses are expected to decline
from 5.4% on average total assets in 1971 to 3.8%oZ in 1972 and, with the ex-
panding operations, reach a still rather high 2.9% in 1976 reflecting the
somewhat top-heavy management structure that Caribe expects to have. Over-
a1l, these projections seem attainable.
29.       Caribe intends to retain most of its future earnings.  It declared
a 57 from 1972 earnings, equivalent to a 30% pay-out. Dividends projected
for 1974, 1975 and 1976, are equivalent to a very low 1.2%, 2.0%, and 2.7%
of par value, respectively. As a result, the highest pay-out ratio would
only be 16%. Consequently, reserves and unappropriated surplus would in-
crease from ColS 5.6 million in 1971, to Col$ 23.8 million in 1976, equiv-
alent to a satisfactory 11% of total portfolio in 1976. Due to the low
dividends projected, book value of shares would increase from 128% of par
in 1971 to 179.3% of par in 1976.
30.       Caribe does not project full utilization of its borrowing capacity
with its debt to equity ratio increasing from 0.9:1 in 1971 to 3.6:1 at the
end of 1976. Its policy statement specifies a debt equity limit of 20:1.



APPENDIX G
Page 7
If Caribe is to participate in the proposed loan, like the other new finan-
cieras it would need to limit this ratio to 3:1 until its net worth exceeds
Col$ 35 million, and to 4:1 thereafter. Caribe has indicated it would change
the debt/equity provision to 3:1 if it becomes eligible for Bank funds.
31.       Caribe's debt service coverage implied by the projections, would be
satisfactory. During the projected period, collections on loans are ex-
pected to amount to a minimum of 2.4 times the amount of repayments on debts.
Profits before financial expenses will be more than 1.4 times interest pay-
able.
CONCLUSIONS AND RECOMMENDATIONS
32.       Conclusions.  Caribe is controlled by foreign interests, in partic-
ular by the Dutch Algemene Bank group. The financiera has been predominantly
extending short-term working capital loans based on the board's knowledge of
the borrower or of the security put up for the loan.  Its experience in formal
appraisal of long-term investments is therefore very limited. W4hile manage-
ment on the presidential and vice-presidential levels and staff have changed,
Caribe lacks effective management, and sufficient staff and experience. Its
appraisal capacity -- as shown in recent evaluations of long-term proposals --
and follow-up capability are not yet adequate for term lending. The same is
true of its disbursement and procurement procedures. Apparently, Caribe in-
tends to introduce procedures laid down in an operating manual to overcome
the shortcomings.
33.       Caribe's financial position is solid.  Its portfolio is of good
quality; arrearage minimal and its loans are generally adequately covered by
collateral. Portfolio coverage by reserves is more than adequate. Due to high
adninistrative costs and low financial leverage, Caribe's profitability has
remained somewhat low, but has shown a tendency to increase.
34.       Prospects for growing short and long-term operations are good pro-
vided that Caribe obtains effective management and adequate staff. If Caribe
is given access to the proposed Bank loan, it would enable it to embark on
long-term financing on a larger scale.
35.       Recommendations.  Given its present lack of adequate management,
limited capability for appraisal and follow-up and organization shortcomings,
Caribe's participation in the proposed loan could only be recommended if
BR's reappraisal shows: (a) the installation of effective management; (b)
demonstration of adequate project appraisal and supervision capacity; (c)
adequate internal procedures, including those for procurement disbursement;
and (d) introduction of 3:1 debt/equity ratio in Caribe's Policy Statement.
